The Mu'tazilite Definition of 'Ilah' and Its First Proof
Mu'tazilites define Ilah as deserving worship and argue against jabr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definition of 'Ilah' as the one deserving worship, and their argument that this definition is incompatible with predestination.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites define Ilah as deserving worship, which they use to argue against predestination.

The Mu'tazilites begin by defining 'Ilah' in a way that seems natural—God deserves worship. But they build a case that this definition forces a rejection of predestination (al-jabr). This passage presents their two key arguments, including a citation from the Mu'tazilite scholar al-Jubba'i.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite claim and first argument
Read the passage where the Mu'tazilites state their definition and link it to jabr. Focus on the first argument: if God creates disbelief and punishes, He is not beneficent and thus not deserving of worship.
Notice how they use the threat of apostasy to undermine the idea of deserving worship.
2
Examine the second argument about inability
The second argument: if the servant cannot perform worship, then only God can worship Himself, which is absurd. This forces the conclusion that the servant must have free will.
This argument directly attacks the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb).
3
Consider the al-Jubba'i quote
Al-Jubba'i's interpretation of the Quranic verse shows how the Mu'tazilites saw predestination as incompatible with God's praiseworthiness.
This illustrates the theological stakes behind a seemingly linguistic definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة : الإله هو الذي تحق له العبادة وهذا المعنى لازم على القول بالجبر . وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : إنه تعالى إنما يكون مستحقاً للعبادة ، لأنه صدر عنه غاية الإنعام والإحسان . ولو أنه خلق فيهم الكفر ، ليستدرجهم إلى النار ، لما كان منعماً على العبيد . وأيضا : فكل من آمن في الحال، لم يأمن أن تكون عاقبة أمره : الردة والكفر . وحينئذ يصير كل ما هو فيه وبالاً عليه . ومن كان كذلك ، فهو أعظم المفترين ، لأعظم المنعمين. فكيف يستحق العبادة والتعظيم ؟ ولهذا السبب قال الجبائي في تفسير قوله : نعم المولى ونعم النصير : إن كان تعالى يخلق الكفر في الكافر في الدنيا ثم يعذبه عليه أبد الآباد في الآخرة ، فحينئذ لا يكون هو - تعالى - نعم المولى ونعم النصير بل يكون : بئس المولى وبئس النصير . الثاني : إنه - تعالى - إنما يستحق العبادة على العبد ، لو كان العبد قادراً على فعل العبادة . أما إذا كان العبد غير قادر على العبادة . بل يكون القادر على العبادة هو الله تعالى لزم أن يقال : إنه استحق على نفسه أن يعبد نفسه ، وذلك غير معقول قال أهل السنة : كلامكم مبني على أن لفظ الله ، معناه : المستحق للعبادة . وهذا باطل . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنا بينا في تفسير أسماء الله : أن « الله » : اسم علم غير مشتق الثاني : لو سلمنا أنه مشتق ، لكن لا نسلم أن معناه : أنه الذي يستحق العبادة . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنه لو كان كذلك ، لوجب أن لا يكون إلهاً للجمادات والبهائم ، لأنه - تعالى - يستحق عليها العبادة ، ولزم أن لا يكون إلهاً في الأزل لم يخلق أحداً ، ولم ينعم على أحد فلم يكن مستحقاً للعبادة الثاني : إنا بينا : أن العقل لا يدل على حصول الاستحقاق . لأنه لا يتفاوت حال المعبود بسبب هذه العبادة - وهي شاقة على العابد - فوجب أن لا يحكم العقل بوجوبه إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : الإله هو القادر على الاختراع . والدليل عليه : أنه تعالى إنما ظهر الامتياز بينه وبين سائر الذوات بصفته الخلاقية . فقال : أفمن يخلق كمن لا يخلق ؟ ولما كان المذكور في معرض الامتياز هو هذه الصفة ، علمنا : أن معني الإلهية ليس إلا الخلاقية . فلو كان العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لكان خالقاً لها ، وحينئذ يبطل الفرق بين الإله ، وبين العبد في
The Mu'tazilites said: al-Ilah is the one to whom worship is due. And this meaning is necessary on the doctrine of predetermination (al-jabr). Its explanation is from two aspects: First: He, exalted, only deserves worship because from Him proceeds utmost grace and beneficence. But if He created disbelief in them (the servants) to lead them gradually to the Fire, then He would not be beneficent to the servants. Also: every one who believes at present is not safe from that his final outcome might be apostasy and disbelief. In that case everything that he was upon would become a burden upon him. Whoever is like that is the greatest slanderer against the greatest benefactor. So how can He deserve worship and glorification? For this reason al-Jubba'i said in the interpretation of His statement "What an excellent Master and what an excellent Helper": if He, exalted, creates disbelief in the disbeliever in this world and then punishes him for it forever in the afterlife, then He, exalted, would not be "what an excellent Master and what an excellent Helper" but rather "what an evil master and what an evil helper." Second: He, exalted, deserves worship from the servant only if the servant is able to perform worship. But if the servant is not able to perform worship, rather the one able to worship is God, then it would be necessary to say that He deserves from Himself to worship Himself, and that is not reasonable. The people of the Sunna said: Your statement is based on the word "Allah" meaning the one deserving worship. This is false, and two aspects indicate it: First: we have shown in the interpretation of the names of God that "Allah" is a proper name, not derived. Second: if we grant that it is derived, we do not grant that its meaning is the one who deserves worship. Two aspects indicate that: First: if that were so, then it would be necessary that He is not an ilah for inanimate objects and animals, because He, exalted, deserves worship from them, and it would be necessary that He is not an ilah in pre-eternity when He had not created anyone and had not bestowed favor on anyone, so He would not be deserving of worship. Second: we have shown that the intellect does not indicate the attainment of deservingness. Because the state of the worshipped does not change due to this worship—while it is burdensome on the worshiper—so it is necessary that the intellect does not rule it as obligatory. When this is established, we say: al-Ilah is the one powerful over creation. The proof for it is that He, exalted, only manifested the distinction between Himself and other essences by His attribute of creatorship. He said: "Is He who creates like one who does not create?" And since this attribute is mentioned in the context of distinction, we know that the meaning of divinity (al-ilahiyya) is nothing other than creatorship. So if the servant were the originator of his own actions, he would be a creator of them, and then the distinction between al-Ilah and the servant would be nullified in
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents a debate between the Mu'tazilites and the Sunnis (Ash'aris) over the definition of "Ilah". The Mu'tazilites define Ilah as the one who deserves worship, and they argue that this definition is incompatible with the doctrine of predestination (al-jabr) because if God predestines disbelief, He would not be deserving of worship. They give two proofs: first, if God creates disbelief and then punishes, He is not beneficent; second, if the servant cannot perform worship, then only God can worship Himself, which is absurd. The Sunnis respond by refuting the Mu'tazilite definition of the word "Allah". They first assert that "Allah" is a proper name, not derived from any root. Second, even if derived, it does not mean "deserving worship". They provide two arguments: (i) if it meant that, then inanimate objects and animals would also owe worship, and God in pre-eternity would not be Ilah; (ii) the intellect does not dictate deservingness because worship is burdensome and does not benefit the worshipped. Then the Sunnis establish their own definition: Ilah is the one powerful over creation (al-qadir ala al-ikhtira'). The proof is that the Quran distinguishes the Creator from other beings, so the essence of divinity is creatorship. Therefore, if humans create their own actions, they would share in the divine attribute and the distinction collapses—thus arguing against Mu'tazilite free will.

Key Terms
الإله
العبادة
الجبر
الاستحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite definition of Ilah is simply linguistic and unrelated to free will.
Correction: They explicitly use the definition to argue against predestination, making it a theological claim about divine justice.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites, what is the meaning of 'Ilah'?
A) The one powerful over creation
B) The one to whom worship is due
C) The one who creates actions in servants
D) The one who is worshipped by all

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites define Ilah as deserving worship, and they argue that this definition logically requires rejecting predestination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly worship God as the One who deserves worship because of His grace, or do you sometimes feel that your actions are forced and thus shift responsibility away from yourself? How does this affect your sincerity in worship?
?
If you believed that all your actions were predestined, would your worship become mechanical? Reflect on the Mu'tazilite argument: does predestination undermine gratitude? In your own heart, do you feel genuine gratitude for your ability to choose to worship?
?
The Mu'tazilites argue that if God creates disbelief and then punishes, He would be an evil master. How does this challenge your understanding of divine justice? Do you ever question God's justice when seeing suffering? How do you reconcile it with predestination, and what does this lesson demand of you in your trust in God?

The Sunni Refutation and Alternative Definition of 'Ilah'
Sunnis refute Mu'tazilite definition and define Ilah as Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunnis refute the Mu'tazilite definition of 'Ilah' and establish their own definition as the Creator.

Core Idea
The Sunnis refute the Mu'tazilite definition by arguing 'Allah' is a proper name not derived, and propose 'power over creation' as the core meaning.

After critiquing the Mu'tazilite definition of 'Ilah', the Sunni scholars present a two-pronged refutation. First, they argue that 'Allah' is a proper name, not derived from any root. Second, even if derived, it does not mean 'deserving worship'. They then provide their own positive definition: Ilah is the one who has power over creation, proven by the Quranic verse distinguishing the Creator from His creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sunni refutation of the Mu'tazilite definition
The Sunnis first argue that 'Allah' is a proper name (ism 'alam), not derived. Then, even assuming derivation, they give two arguments against the meaning 'deserving worship'.
Note the structure: two overall responses, each with two sub-arguments.
2
Examine the two arguments against derivational meaning
First: if 'Ilah' meant 'deserving worship', then inanimate objects would also be required to worship, and God in pre-eternity would not be Ilah. Second: the intellect does not indicate deservingness because worship burdens the worshipper without benefiting God.
These arguments are logical, not scriptural.
3
Learn the Sunni positive definition
The Sunnis define Ilah as the one powerful over creation (al-qadir ala al-ikhtira'), with proof from the Quran: 'Is He who creates like one who does not create?' This shows that the essence of divinity is creatorship.
This definition directly counters the Mu'tazilite emphasis on free will; if humans create their own actions, they share in divinity.

قالت المعتزلة : الإله هو الذي تحق له العبادة وهذا المعنى لازم على القول بالجبر . وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : إنه تعالى إنما يكون مستحقاً للعبادة ، لأنه صدر عنه غاية الإنعام والإحسان . ولو أنه خلق فيهم الكفر ، ليستدرجهم إلى النار ، لما كان منعماً على العبيد . وأيضا : فكل من آمن في الحال، لم يأمن أن تكون عاقبة أمره : الردة والكفر . وحينئذ يصير كل ما هو فيه وبالاً عليه . ومن كان كذلك ، فهو أعظم المفترين ، لأعظم المنعمين. فكيف يستحق العبادة والتعظيم ؟ ولهذا السبب قال الجبائي في تفسير قوله : نعم المولى ونعم النصير : إن كان تعالى يخلق الكفر في الكافر في الدنيا ثم يعذبه عليه أبد الآباد في الآخرة ، فحينئذ لا يكون هو - تعالى - نعم المولى ونعم النصير بل يكون : بئس المولى وبئس النصير . الثاني : إنه - تعالى - إنما يستحق العبادة على العبد ، لو كان العبد قادراً على فعل العبادة . أما إذا كان العبد غير قادر على العبادة . بل يكون القادر على العبادة هو الله تعالى لزم أن يقال : إنه استحق على نفسه أن يعبد نفسه ، وذلك غير معقول قال أهل السنة : كلامكم مبني على أن لفظ الله ، معناه : المستحق للعبادة . وهذا باطل . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنا بينا في تفسير أسماء الله : أن « الله » : اسم علم غير مشتق الثاني : لو سلمنا أنه مشتق ، لكن لا نسلم أن معناه : أنه الذي يستحق العبادة . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إنه لو كان كذلك ، لوجب أن لا يكون إلهاً للجمادات والبهائم ، لأنه - تعالى - يستحق عليها العبادة ، ولزم أن لا يكون إلهاً في الأزل لم يخلق أحداً ، ولم ينعم على أحد فلم يكن مستحقاً للعبادة الثاني : إنا بينا : أن العقل لا يدل على حصول الاستحقاق . لأنه لا يتفاوت حال المعبود بسبب هذه العبادة - وهي شاقة على العابد - فوجب أن لا يحكم العقل بوجوبه إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : الإله هو القادر على الاختراع . والدليل عليه : أنه تعالى إنما ظهر الامتياز بينه وبين سائر الذوات بصفته الخلاقية . فقال : أفمن يخلق كمن لا يخلق ؟ ولما كان المذكور في معرض الامتياز هو هذه الصفة ، علمنا : أن معني الإلهية ليس إلا الخلاقية . فلو كان العبد موجداً لأفعال نفسه ، لكان خالقاً لها ، وحينئذ يبطل الفرق بين الإله ، وبين العبد في
The Mu'tazilites said: al-Ilah is the one to whom worship is due. And this meaning is necessary on the doctrine of predetermination (al-jabr). Its explanation is from two aspects: First: He, exalted, only deserves worship because from Him proceeds utmost grace and beneficence. But if He created disbelief in them (the servants) to lead them gradually to the Fire, then He would not be beneficent to the servants. Also: every one who believes at present is not safe from that his final outcome might be apostasy and disbelief. In that case everything that he was upon would become a burden upon him. Whoever is like that is the greatest slanderer against the greatest benefactor. So how can He deserve worship and glorification? For this reason al-Jubba'i said in the interpretation of His statement "What an excellent Master and what an excellent Helper": if He, exalted, creates disbelief in the disbeliever in this world and then punishes him for it forever in the afterlife, then He, exalted, would not be "what an excellent Master and what an excellent Helper" but rather "what an evil master and what an evil helper." Second: He, exalted, deserves worship from the servant only if the servant is able to perform worship. But if the servant is not able to perform worship, rather the one able to worship is God, then it would be necessary to say that He deserves from Himself to worship Himself, and that is not reasonable. The people of the Sunna said: Your statement is based on the word "Allah" meaning the one deserving worship. This is false, and two aspects indicate it: First: we have shown in the interpretation of the names of God that "Allah" is a proper name, not derived. Second: if we grant that it is derived, we do not grant that its meaning is the one who deserves worship. Two aspects indicate that: First: if that were so, then it would be necessary that He is not an ilah for inanimate objects and animals, because He, exalted, deserves worship from them, and it would be necessary that He is not an ilah in pre-eternity when He had not created anyone and had not bestowed favor on anyone, so He would not be deserving of worship. Second: we have shown that the intellect does not indicate the attainment of deservingness. Because the state of the worshipped does not change due to this worship—while it is burdensome on the worshiper—so it is necessary that the intellect does not rule it as obligatory. When this is established, we say: al-Ilah is the one powerful over creation. The proof for it is that He, exalted, only manifested the distinction between Himself and other essences by His attribute of creatorship. He said: "Is He who creates like one who does not create?" And since this attribute is mentioned in the context of distinction, we know that the meaning of divinity (al-ilahiyya) is nothing other than creatorship. So if the servant were the originator of his own actions, he would be a creator of them, and then the distinction between al-Ilah and the servant would be nullified in
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage presents a debate between the Mu'tazilites and the Sunnis (Ash'aris) over the definition of "Ilah". The Mu'tazilites define Ilah as the one who deserves worship, and they argue that this definition is incompatible with the doctrine of predestination (al-jabr) because if God predestines disbelief, He would not be deserving of worship. They give two proofs: first, if God creates disbelief and then punishes, He is not beneficent; second, if the servant cannot perform worship, then only God can worship Himself, which is absurd. The Sunnis respond by refuting the Mu'tazilite definition of the word "Allah". They first assert that "Allah" is a proper name, not derived from any root. Second, even if derived, it does not mean "deserving worship". They provide two arguments: (i) if it meant that, then inanimate objects and animals would also owe worship, and God in pre-eternity would not be Ilah; (ii) the intellect does not dictate deservingness because worship is burdensome and does not benefit the worshipped. Then the Sunnis establish their own definition: Ilah is the one powerful over creation (al-qadir ala al-ikhtira'). The proof is that the Quran distinguishes the Creator from other beings, so the essence of divinity is creatorship. Therefore, if humans create their own actions, they would share in the divine attribute and the distinction collapses—thus arguing against Mu'tazilite free will.

العبادة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the debate is merely about the etymology of the word 'Allah'.
Correction: The debate is fundamentally about the nature of divine attributes and the implication for human free will; the Sunnis' definition of Ilah as Creator directly refutes Mu'tazilite free will.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunnis in this passage, what is the correct meaning of 'Ilah'?
A) The one deserving worship
B) The one powerful over creation
C) The one who creates actions in servants
D) The one who is worshipped by all

Key Takeaway
The Sunnis define 'Ilah' as the Creator, refuting the Mu'tazilite definition based on deservingness, thereby defending divine uniqueness against human free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your daily struggles and achievements, do you genuinely feel that every breath, every thought, and every action is given by the Creator alone? Or do you secretly take credit for your own 'creations'? This lesson demolishes the illusion of self-creation—will you now surrender every part of your life to Allah as the sole Creator and Sovereign?
?
Imagine yourself before Allah on the Day of Judgment, standing with nothing but His mercy. Did your worship ever make you feel entitled to Paradise? This lesson teaches that Allah deserves worship not because you benefit Him, but because He is the Creator. How will you change your worship to be purely out of recognition of His right, not your own expectation?
?
If you were to claim any power over your life independent of Allah—even the power to choose to read this lesson—you would be blurring the line between Creator and created. What specific area of your life do you most need to admit that you are not in control, and how will you actively submit that to Allah today?

Mu'tazila Objection on Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim and the Sunni Response
Mu'tazila objection and partial Sunni response regarding divine names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that God's names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim are incompatible with predestination, and the initial Sunni rebuttal based on the dilemma of power and motive.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila's objection against predestination backfires through the dilemma of necessary versus coincidental acts.

In this passage, the Mu'tazila argue that if all actions are from God, then God's punishment outweighs His mercy, contradicting His names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim. They present their own view that evil comes from the servant's choice. The Sunni response turns the tables by exposing a dilemma in the Mu'tazila's own position regarding power and motive.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila objection
Carefully read the Mu'tazila's argument that God's mercy is contradicted if He creates disbelief. Understand their supporting verses.
2
Identify the Sunni response's first aspect
Find where Ahl al-Sunna begin their rebuttal. They present a dilemma about power and motive.
The passage cuts off, but the dilemma is clear.
3
Analyze the dilemma
Consider the two options: either the act is necessary given power and motive, or it is not. Both lead to problems for the Mu'tazila.
4
Reflect on the incompleteness
Note that the full Sunni argument is not present; only the first aspect is given. The lesson presents this partial argument.
This is due to the source cutting off.

الفصل الثالث في المباحث الواقعة في قولنا الرحمن الرحيم . قالت المعتزلة : لو كان الكفر والفسق كله من الله ، لا من العبد ، لكان المغضوب عليه من الخلق ، أكثر من المرحوم . لأن أهل النار أكثر من أهل الجنة . ومن كان عقابه وضرره أكثر من رحمته وإحسانه ، لم يكن رحماناً . لأن الرحمن هو الكامل في الرحمة، والرحيم من صفة الرحمة بشرط أن لا يحصل نقيضه . قالوا : فأما على مذهبنا فإنه يتم لنا القول بأنه الرحمن الرحيم ، لأنه تعالى خلق الخلق كلهم للنفع والرحمة، وأما الذين كفروا وفسقوا فمن جهة أنفسهم . ونقول في ذلك كما قال تعالى : ( إن الله لا يغير ما بقوم ، حتى يغيروا ما بأنفسهم (١)) وقال : ما يفعل الله بعذابكم . إن شكرتم وآمنتم (۲) ) وقال : ( قل : ما يعبثوا بكم ربي . لولا دعاؤكم ) (۳) فثبت : آن کونه رحمانا رحيماً لا يثبت إلا على قولنا . قال أهل السنة والجماعة : هذا الذي أوردتم علينا ، وارد عليكم وبيانه من وجوه : الأول : عند حصول القدرة والداعية . إما أن يجب الفعل ، أو لا يجب . فإن وجب ، لزمكم ما ألزمتموه علينا . لأنه تعالى هو الخالق لتلك القدرة ولتلك الداعية . وهما يوجبان الكفر والفسق . فكان ذلك إجبار للعبد على القبح . وأما إن قلتم : إن عند حصول القدرة والداعية لا يجب الفعل فحينئذ يكون صدور الفعل اتفاقياً ، والاتفاقي لا يكون مقدوراً للعبد . فيترتب
Third chapter on investigations concerning our saying 'Ar-Rahman Ar-Rahim'. The Mu'tazila said: If disbelief and sin were entirely from God, not from the servant, then those of creation with wrath upon them would be more than the mercifully treated. Because the people of the Fire are more than the people of Paradise. And whoever's punishment and harm are more than His mercy and beneficence, he would not be Rahman. Because Ar-Rahman is the perfect in mercy, and Ar-Rahim is an attribute of mercy with the condition that its opposite does not occur. They said: As for our doctrine, the saying that He is Ar-Rahman Ar-Rahim is established for us, because He the Exalted created all of creation for benefit and mercy; as for those who disbelieve and sin, it is from their own selves. And we say in that as Allah the Exalted said: 'Indeed, Allah does not change the condition of a people until they change what is in themselves.' And He said: 'What would Allah do with your punishment if you are grateful and believe?' And He said: 'Say: My Lord would not care for you were it not for your supplication.' So it is established that His being Rahman and Rahim is only established according to our doctrine. Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: This which you have brought against us is brought back against you. Its explanation is from several aspects: The first: When power and motive are present, either the act is necessary or not necessary. If it is necessary, then what you have charged us with applies to you. Because He the Exalted is the creator of that power and that motive, and they necessitate disbelief and sin. So that would be compulsion of the servant to evil. And if you say that when power and motive are present the act is not necessary, then the occurrence of the act would be coincidental, and the coincidental is not within the servant's power. So it follows...
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer incompletely presented. It begins with the Mu'tazila objection: if all actions (including disbelief and sin) are from God, then God punishes more than He rewards, contradicting the names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim. They support their view that evil comes from the servant's own choice, citing Quranic verses. Then Ahl al-Sunna respond by turning the argument back on the Mu'tazila. They present the first of several aspects: the problem of power and motive. If the Mu'tazila hold that when power and motive are present, the act becomes necessary, then since God creates both power and motive, He would be compelling the servant to evil – the very charge they leveled against the Sunnis. If instead they say the act is not necessary, then the act would be coincidental, which cannot be within the servant's power. The passage is cut off here, but the argument is that the Mu'tazila cannot avoid the dilemma.

Correction: The Sunni response shows that the Mu'tazila face the same dilemma: their own position either leads to compulsion or to an unintelligible accidental act.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that the Mu'tazila objection fails; but more importantly, does your heart rest in God's mercy despite not fully understanding His decree? What would change in your life if you truly believed that His mercy is not diminished by allowing disbelief?
?
Consider the dilemma: either God compels or actions are accidental. The Sunnis affirm that God creates actions but man acquires them. Are you comfortable with this mystery? Do you ever feel that your sins are 'coincidental'—something that just happened—rather than taking full responsibility? This lesson challenges you to examine: are you truly changing what is in yourself, or are you using theological arguments as an excuse?

The Absurdity of Commanding Against God's Knowledge
First part of second Sunni proof: absurdity of commanding against God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why commanding a person God knows will not obey is rationally absurd and incompatible with mercy.

Core Idea
Commanding against God's knowledge is like asking to change the unchangeable.

This lesson examines the second Sunni proof against the Mu'tazila. The argument builds on the premise that God's knowledge is infallible: what He knows must happen, and its opposite is impossible. Therefore, commanding a person He knows will die in disbelief to believe is effectively asking him to change God's knowledge—an absurdity that cannot be merciful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument starts with the impossibility of opposing God's knowledge.
The passage uses 'خلاف معلوم الله' (opposite of what God knows).
2
Identify the core claim
The claim is that commanding a known disbeliever to believe is like saying 'Change God's knowledge or I will punish you.' This is the absurd consequence.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: if God's knowledge is fixed, can a command to do otherwise be meaningful or merciful? The Sunni answer is no.
This challenges the Mu'tazila view that God must command what is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن خلاف معلوم الله ممتنع الوقوع . فإذا قال لمن علم أنه يموت على الكفر : كلفتك بالإيمان. فكأنه قال له : غير علم الله وإلا عاقبتك . ومعلوم أن هذا لا يليق بالرحمة البتة . لأن كل ما أوصل الله من الرحمات إلى عباده ، لا يخلو إما أن يقال : إنه تعالى أحوجهم إليها وجعلهم مشتهين لها ، أو ما فعل ذلك . فإن كان الأول لم يكن ذلك رحمة . لأنه تعالى لما أحوجهم إليها ، ثم أعطاهم ذلك المحتاج إليه ، جرى هذا مجرى ما إذا خرق بطن إنسان ثم يداويه . فإن ذلك لا يعد رحمة. وإن كان الثاني وهو أنه تعالى ما أحوجهم إليها ، وما جعلهم مشتهين لها ، راغبين في حصولها ، لم يكن إيصال ذلك نفعاً في حقهم. ألا ترى أن إلقاء العظم إلى الكلب إحسان إليه . لأنه يشتهي ذلك العظم . أما إلقاء قلادة من الدر إليه ، فإنه لا يكون إحساناً إليه . لأنه لا يشتهي ذلك فثبت أن على القسمين لا تعقل حصول الرحمة. وهذا بخلاف الإحسان الصادر من الخلق ، لأن الحاجة ما حصلت من قبل هذا المحسن ، بل كانت الحاجة موجودة في ذات ذلك الشخص . فإذا سعى إنسان في دفع تلك الحاجة ، كان ذلك إحساناً . أما الحق سبحانه فهو الذي أحوج إلى ذلك الشخص ، فإذا أعطى المحتاج إليه ، جرى ذلك مجرى ما إذا أساء ثم أحسن بقدر تلك الإساءة ، ومجرى ما إذا جرح ثم عالج ، فإن ذلك لا يكون إحساناً . فثبت : أن هذا الإلزام وارد عليهم
The second: Verily, the opposite of what God knows is impossible to occur. So when He says to the one whom He knows will die in disbelief: 'I charge you with faith,' it is as if He said to him: 'Change God's knowledge, or I will punish you.' And it is known that this is not suitable for mercy at all. Because all the mercies that God conveys to His servants are either such that it is said: He Almighty made them need them and made them desire them, or He did not do that. If the first, then that is not mercy. Because when He made them need them, then gave them what they needed, this is like someone who rips open a person's belly and then treats it—that is not considered mercy. And if the second, that He Almighty did not make them need them, and did not make them desire them or yearn for their acquisition, then conveying that is not a benefit to them. Do you not see that throwing a bone to a dog is kindness to it because it desires that bone? As for throwing a necklace of pearls to it, it is not kindness to it because it does not desire it. So it is established that in both cases, the occurrence of mercy is not conceivable. This is unlike beneficence from creatures, because the need did not arise from this benefactor; rather the need existed in the person himself. So if a person strives to remove that need, that is beneficence. As for the Truth, exalted, He is the one who made that person needy, so if He gives him what he needs, this is like one who does evil then does good to the extent of that evil, and like one who wounds then treats—that is not beneficence. So it is established that this objection applies to them.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is the second Sunni response. It consists of two main points. First, the Sunni scholars argue that God's knowledge is infallible: what God knows will happen must happen, and its opposite is impossible. Commanding a person He knows will die in disbelief to believe is effectively asking him to change God's knowledge, which is absurd and not merciful. Second, they analyze the concept of mercy (rahma) itself. When God grants something to a servant, either He created the need and desire for it in that servant, or He did not. If He created the need, then giving what is needed is like causing harm and then repairing it—not true mercy. If He did not create the need, then the gift is useless, like throwing pearls to a dog that desires bones. In either case, it cannot be mercy. Unlike human beneficence, where the need already exists independently of the benefactor, God is the originator of the need. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's objection that God's mercy is compromised by predestination actually applies to their own view.

Key Terms
معلوم الله
التكليف
الرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can command something He knows will not happen without contradiction.
Correction: God's knowledge is immutable; commanding against it is absurd and not merciful.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does commanding a known disbeliever to believe implicitly demand?
A) That the person obey
B) That God change His knowledge
C) That the person be punished
D) That mercy be shown

Key Takeaway
Commanding what God knows will not happen is rationally absurd and cannot be merciful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a situation where you desperately wanted something that you knew deep down was not meant for you. How does the truth that God's knowledge is unchangeable affect the way you pray and surrender?
?
This lesson shows that it is absurd to command someone to change God's knowledge. In what areas of your life are you still trying to 'change God's knowledge' by resisting His decree? What would it mean for you to accept His knowledge with trust?
?
If mercy cannot be built on an absurd demand, then what kind of mercy does God actually offer you? How can you stop demanding that God be merciful in your terms, and instead receive His mercy as He has defined it?

Redefining Mercy: the Dog Analogy
Second part of proof: analysis of mercy and analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni argument that true mercy requires the recipient to have a genuine need and desire for the gift.

Core Idea
True mercy requires need and desire; otherwise it's a useless gift.

After establishing the absurdity of commanding against God's knowledge, this lesson unpacks the deeper analysis of mercy. The passage argues that for a gift to be merciful, it must either be needed and desired (but then God created that need, making the gift like repairing a self-inflicted wound) or not needed (making it useless, like pearls to a dog). This redefinition shows that the Mu'tazila's understanding of mercy is flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage on mercy
Read the Arabic passage from 'لأن كل ما أوصل الله' to the end. Notice the two cases and the analogy.
2
Understand the two cases
Case 1: God creates the need and then fulfills it – like ripping a belly then treating it, not mercy. Case 2: God does not create the need – then the gift is not beneficial, like pearls to a dog.
3
Contrast with human beneficence
Unlike humans, God is the originator of needs. So giving what He Himself made necessary is not mercy but repair of His own doing.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that the Mu'tazila objection actually applies to their own view. Mercy is redefined consistently with God's sovereignty.

الثاني : إن خلاف معلوم الله ممتنع الوقوع . فإذا قال لمن علم أنه يموت على الكفر : كلفتك بالإيمان. فكأنه قال له : غير علم الله وإلا عاقبتك . ومعلوم أن هذا لا يليق بالرحمة البتة . لأن كل ما أوصل الله من الرحمات إلى عباده ، لا يخلو إما أن يقال : إنه تعالى أحوجهم إليها وجعلهم مشتهين لها ، أو ما فعل ذلك . فإن كان الأول لم يكن ذلك رحمة . لأنه تعالى لما أحوجهم إليها ، ثم أعطاهم ذلك المحتاج إليه ، جرى هذا مجرى ما إذا خرق بطن إنسان ثم يداويه . فإن ذلك لا يعد رحمة. وإن كان الثاني وهو أنه تعالى ما أحوجهم إليها ، وما جعلهم مشتهين لها ، راغبين في حصولها ، لم يكن إيصال ذلك نفعاً في حقهم. ألا ترى أن إلقاء العظم إلى الكلب إحسان إليه . لأنه يشتهي ذلك العظم . أما إلقاء قلادة من الدر إليه ، فإنه لا يكون إحساناً إليه . لأنه لا يشتهي ذلك فثبت أن على القسمين لا تعقل حصول الرحمة. وهذا بخلاف الإحسان الصادر من الخلق ، لأن الحاجة ما حصلت من قبل هذا المحسن ، بل كانت الحاجة موجودة في ذات ذلك الشخص . فإذا سعى إنسان في دفع تلك الحاجة ، كان ذلك إحساناً . أما الحق سبحانه فهو الذي أحوج إلى ذلك الشخص ، فإذا أعطى المحتاج إليه ، جرى ذلك مجرى ما إذا أساء ثم أحسن بقدر تلك الإساءة ، ومجرى ما إذا جرح ثم عالج ، فإن ذلك لا يكون إحساناً . فثبت : أن هذا الإلزام وارد عليهم
The second: Verily, the opposite of what God knows is impossible to occur. So when He says to the one whom He knows will die in disbelief: 'I charge you with faith,' it is as if He said to him: 'Change God's knowledge, or I will punish you.' And it is known that this is not suitable for mercy at all. Because all the mercies that God conveys to His servants are either such that it is said: He Almighty made them need them and made them desire them, or He did not do that. If the first, then that is not mercy. Because when He made them need them, then gave them what they needed, this is like someone who rips open a person's belly and then treats it—that is not considered mercy. And if the second, that He Almighty did not make them need them, and did not make them desire them or yearn for their acquisition, then conveying that is not a benefit to them. Do you not see that throwing a bone to a dog is kindness to it because it desires that bone? As for throwing a necklace of pearls to it, it is not kindness to it because it does not desire it. So it is established that in both cases, the occurrence of mercy is not conceivable. This is unlike beneficence from creatures, because the need did not arise from this benefactor; rather the need existed in the person himself. So if a person strives to remove that need, that is beneficence. As for the Truth, exalted, He is the one who made that person needy, so if He gives him what he needs, this is like one who does evil then does good to the extent of that evil, and like one who wounds then treats—that is not beneficence. So it is established that this objection applies to them.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is the second Sunni response. It consists of two main points. First, the Sunni scholars argue that God's knowledge is infallible: what God knows will happen must happen, and its opposite is impossible. Commanding a person He knows will die in disbelief to believe is effectively asking him to change God's knowledge, which is absurd and not merciful. Second, they analyze the concept of mercy (rahma) itself. When God grants something to a servant, either He created the need and desire for it in that servant, or He did not. If He created the need, then giving what is needed is like causing harm and then repairing it—not true mercy. If He did not create the need, then the gift is useless, like throwing pearls to a dog that desires bones. In either case, it cannot be mercy. Unlike human beneficence, where the need already exists independently of the benefactor, God is the originator of the need. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's objection that God's mercy is compromised by predestination actually applies to their own view.

Key Terms
الرحمة
الحاجة
الإحسان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That mercy means simply giving something good regardless of the recipient's state.
Correction: True mercy requires the recipient to have a genuine need and desire; otherwise it's not mercy.

Checkpoint
In the dog analogy, why is throwing a pearl necklace not kindness?
A) Because pearls are worthless
B) Because the dog does not desire pearls
C) Because the necklace is too heavy
D) Because dogs only like bones

Key Takeaway
Mercy from God requires genuine need and desire; otherwise, it is either repair of self-inflicted harm or useless gift.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing from God, do you ever stop to ask yourself whether you truly desired it, or are you simply taking it as a routine provision? How does the dog-pearl analogy challenge your perception of God's gifts?
?
Think of a time you felt God was unjust because He gave you something you didn't ask for. Does this passage convict you of ingratitude? How can you shift from entitlement to recognizing that even the creation of your need is a divine act?
?
The passage says God's mercy is not like human beneficence. How does that change your reliance on God? Are you ready to accept that your very neediness is from Him, and that every gift is a response to a need He created? What would it mean for you to live in full awareness of this dependency?

Fourth Refutation: Does God's Foreknowledge Make Creation Futile?
Fourth proof: God's knowledge of the disbeliever's fate undermines mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial premise of the fourth Sunni refutation against the Mu'tazilite claim that creation and obligation are merciful.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge of disbelief shows creation is not purely merciful.

This lesson introduces the fourth argument presented by Sunni theologians against the Mu'tazilite assertion that God's creation and obligation are fundamentally merciful. The argument begins with a simple yet profound premise: God knows from eternity that certain individuals will never believe and will only gain eternal punishment. This challenges the notion that creation for such individuals is an act of mercy. The passage is incomplete, but the direction of reasoning is clear.

The argument likely concludes that such creation and obligation cannot be attributed to a merciful God if they only lead to punishment. Refuting the Mu'tazila on Divine Mercy
The author refutes the Mu'tazila's claim that commanding the unbeliever is unjust, using the impossibility of changing God's knowledge and analyzing the concept of mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Mu'tazila's claim that commanding the unbeliever is unjust, using the impossibility of changing God's knowledge and analyzing the concept of mercy.

Core Idea
God's mercy cannot be defined by human standards of kindness.

This passage tackles a key Mu'tazila argument against the Ash'ari view that God commands the unbeliever while knowing he will disbelieve. The author deconstructs what 'mercy' means under their own assumptions and shows it becomes self-defeating. Let's follow his logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument.
Pay attention to the two cases the author presents.
2
Identify the initial premise
Notice the author begins by stating that what is contrary to God's knowledge cannot occur. This is the foundation of the refutation.
3
Follow the two-case analysis of mercy
The author splits the analysis into two possibilities: either God creates the need for mercy or He does not. Understand why each case fails to constitute genuine mercy.
Use the analogies of tearing a stomach and throwing a pearl necklace to a dog.
4
Contrast with human kindness
The author contrasts God's action with human kindness, where the need pre-exists independently. Grasp why this distinction undermines the Mu'tazila's objection.
5
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that the Mu'tazila's objection applies to them, not to the Ash'ari position. Reflect on the overall argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن خلاف معلوم الله ممتنع الوقوع . فإذا قال لمن علم أنه يموت على الكفر : كلفتك بالإيمان. فكأنه قال له : غير علم الله وإلا عاقبتك . ومعلوم أن هذا لا يليق بالرحمة .البتة . لأن كل ما أوصل الله من الرحمات إلى عباده ، لا يخلو إما أن يقال : إنه تعالى أحوجهم إليها وجعلهم مشتهين لها ، أو ما فعل ذلك . فإن كان الأول لم يكن ذلك رحمة . لأنه تعالى لما أحوجهم إليها ، ثم أعطاهم ذلك المحتاج إليه ، جرى هذا مجرى ما إذا خرق بطن إنسان ثم يداويه . فإن ذلك لا يعد رحمة. وإن كان الثاني وهو أنه تعالى ما أحوجهم إليها ، وما جعلهم مشتهين لها ، راغبين في حصولها ، لم يكن إيصال ذلك نفعاً في حقهم. ألا ترى أن إلقاء العظم إلى الكلب إحسان إليه . لأنه يشتهي ذلك العظم . أما إلقاء قلادة من الدر إليه ، فإنه لا يكون إحساناً إليه. لأنه لا يشتهي ذلك فثبت أن على القسمين لا تعقل حصول الرحمة. وهذا بخلاف الإحسان الصادر من الخلق ، لأن الحاجة ما حصلت من قبل هذا المحسن ، بل كانت الحاجة موجودة في ذات ذلك الشخص . فإذا سعى إنسان في دفع تلك الحاجة ، كان ذلك إحساناً . أما الحق سبحانه فهو الذي أحوج إلى ذلك الشخص ، فإذا أعطى المحتاج إليه ، جرى ذلك مجرى ما إذا أساء ثم أحسن بقدر تلك الإساءة ، ومجرى ما إذا جرح ثم عالج ، فإن ذلك لا يكون إحساناً . فثبت : أن هذا الإلزام وارد عليهم
Second: Indeed, what is contrary to God's knowledge is impossible to occur. So if He says to one whom He knows will die upon unbelief: 'I have obligated you to believe'—it is as if He said to him: 'Change God's knowledge, or else I will punish you.' And it is known that this is not befitting of mercy at all. Because everything that God conveys of mercies to His servants is not free from either: it is said that He, exalted, made them in need of them and made them desirous of them, or He did not do that. If the first, then that is not mercy, because when He made them in need of them and then gave them what they needed, this is analogous to someone tearing open a person's belly and then treating it—that is not considered mercy. And if the second—that He, exalted, did not make them in need of them nor make them desirous of them and eager for their attainment—then conveying that to them would not be beneficial for them. Do you not see that throwing a bone to a dog is kindness to it because it desires that bone; but throwing a necklace of pearls to it is not kindness because it does not desire that? So it is established that in both cases the attainment of mercy is inconceivable. This is unlike kindness coming from creatures, because the need did not originate from this benefactor; rather the need existed in the very essence of that person. So if a person strives to remove that need, that is kindness. As for the Truth, exalted, it is He who made that person needy, so if He gives what is needed, this is analogous to one who does evil and then does good to the extent of that evil, and analogous to one who wounds and then treats—that is not kindness. So it is established that this objection applies to them.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument refuting the Mu'tazila's claim that God's punishment of the unbeliever is unjust. The author begins by establishing that what contradicts God's knowledge cannot occur. Therefore, if God commands someone He knows will die in unbelief to believe, it is as if He is demanding the impossible—changing His knowledge—which is unbecoming of mercy. The author then analyzes the nature of God's mercies, positing two possibilities: either God creates the need for mercy in the creature, or He does not. If He creates the need, then fulfilling it is like tearing a person's stomach and then treating it—not true mercy. If He does not create the need, then the gift is not beneficial, like giving a pearl necklace to a dog that desires only a bone. Thus, on either scenario, genuine mercy is impossible under the Mu'tazila's premises. The author contrasts this with human kindness, where the need pre-exists independently of the benefactor, making the act truly kind. Since God creates the very need He fulfills, His act is analogous to doing harm and then repairing it—not kindness. Therefore, the Mu'tazila's objection against the Ash'ari view actually applies to them.

Key Terms
خلاف معلوم الله
الرحمة
الإحسان
الحاجة
المحتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is denying God's mercy or claiming that God's actions are not truly merciful.
Correction: The author is not denying God's mercy; rather, he is showing that under the Mu'tazila's own premises, the concept of mercy becomes inconceivable. The Ash'ari view affirms God's mercy as an attribute, but the Mu'tazila's definition leads to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, if God makes a person needy and then gives him what he needs, why is this not considered mercy?
A) Because the person does not desire it
B) Because God is not obligated to give
C) Because it is analogous to tearing a stomach and then treating it
D) Because it is impossible

Key Takeaway
Under Mu'tazila premises, divine mercy becomes inconceivable because either God creates the need and His gift resembles harm-repair, or He does not create the need and the gift is not beneficial.

The Fourth Argument: God's Knowledge of the Unbeliever's Fate Makes Obligation Harmful
A refutation of the Mu'tazila using the harmful outcome of obligation on the unbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila's assumptions about divine mercy lead to a contradiction when considering the fate of the unbeliever.

Core Idea
God's foreknowledge of the unbeliever's fate makes creating and obligating him harmful, not merciful.

The author presents a refutation of the Mu'tazila by drawing out a consequence of their own premises. If God must act for the creature's benefit, then creating an unbeliever whom He knows will end in eternal punishment is actually causing harm, which contradicts mercy. This argument forces the Mu'tazila to reconcile their view of obligation with divine foreknowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the key claim
Identify why the author considers creating and obligating the unbeliever to be 'striving for harm' and what makes it incompatible with mercy.
Look for the chain from God's knowledge to the act's harmful nature.
3
Understand the implied premise
The argument relies on an unstated premise that mercy precludes causing harm. Consider how this premise is accepted by the Mu'tazila.
This is a logical refutation, not a theological claim.
4
Reflect on the target
Think about how this argument specifically targets the Mu'tazilite view that obligation must benefit the creature. Why does the unbeliever's case expose a contradiction?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنه تعالى لما علم من الكافر أنه لا يقبل الإيمان ، وأنه يقدم على الكفر والفسق ، وأنه لا يستفيد من الخلق والتكليف ، إلا عذاب الأبد . كان خلقه وتكليفه مع علمه بأن عاقبته تؤدي إلى هذه الحالة : سعياً في الإضرار والإيلام . وذلك لا يليق بالرحمة
Fourth: When He, exalted, knows of the unbeliever that he will not accept faith, and that he will proceed upon unbelief and sin, and that he will not benefit from creation and obligation except eternal punishment—His creating and obligating him, while knowing that his end leads to this state, is a striving for harm and causing pain. And that is not befitting of mercy.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This is a short, self-contained argument that refutes the Mu'tazila. The author states that since God knows the unbeliever will not accept faith and will only suffer eternal punishment, creating and obligating him is an act of causing harm and pain, which contradicts mercy. The argument underscores that on the Mu'tazila's own premises (that God must act for the creature's benefit), the actual outcome for the unbeliever is harm, so the action is not merciful.

التكليف

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument claims that God is unjust for creating unbelievers.
Correction: The argument does not claim God is unjust; rather, it shows that the Mu'tazila's own premises about mercy and benefit lead to a contradiction. It is a refutation of their position, not an assertion about God's justice.

Key Takeaway

True Generosity and Mercy: Sunni vs Mu'tazila
The author contrasts the Mu'tazila's view of God's obligation to reward with the Sunni view that God's generosity is purely gratuitous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna consider God's gifts as pure generosity, in contrast to the Mu'tazila view that God is obligated to reward.

Core Idea
Generosity is only true if given without obligation or self-interest.

This passage presents the core Sunni argument that God's mercy and generosity are only genuine when they are not compelled by duty or desire for praise. The author contrasts this with the Mu'tazila position that God must reward obedience. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Pay attention to the definition of generosity (al-jūd) given at the start.
2
Identify the definition of generosity
The passage defines true generosity as 'conferring what is appropriate, without purpose' (إفادة ما ينبغي، لا لغرض). Notice this is the criterion used to evaluate both schools.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila critique
According to the passage, the Mu'tazila claim that God must reward and compensate; otherwise He would deserve blame. Thus His giving is a means to avoid blame and acquire praise – which is a purpose and benefit for Himself. Therefore it fails the definition of generosity.
See how the author shows that Mu'tazila view makes God's act self-interested.
4
Grasp the Sunni counter-argument
The Sunni position is that nothing is obligatory upon God; He is inherently worthy of praise. So His gifts do not affect His status – they do not remove blame nor increase praise. Hence His giving is pure generosity and mercy.
Contrast this with the Mu'tazila: here God's giving has no ulterior motive.
5
Reflect on divine mercy and power
The passage concludes that God is merciful to whom He wills and subjugator to whom He wills, just as He is beneficial and harmful. This balances mercy with power, avoiding the Mu'tazila restriction.
Consider how this affirms both attributes without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أهل السنة والجماعة : الرحمة الكرم إنما تتحقق على مذهبنا ، لا على مذهب المعتزلة . وذلك لأن الجود هو إفادة ما ينبغي ، لا لغرض . وعند المعتزلة أنه تعالى لو لم يعط الثواب والعوض ، فإنه يستحق الذم ، فهو بإعطاء ذلك الثواب ، وذلك العوض ، يتخلص من الذم ، وإذا تفضل فقد اكتسب استحقاق الحمد من العبد . ولولا ذلك التفضل ، لما حصل ذلك الاستحقاق . فثبت : أنه تعالى بإعطاء تلك المنافع قد اكتسب لنفسه منفعة وعوضاً . فلم يكن هذا جوداً . فأما على قولنا : إنه لا يجب على الله لأحد شيئاً ، وأنه مستحق الثناء والحمد لذاته ، لا لأمر منفصل عن ذاته . لم يكن [لفعل] شيء من أنواع الجود والكرم ، أثر في تخليته عن الذم ، ولا أثر في تحصيل مزيد استحقاق الحمد. فكانت عطاياه محض الجود والكرم والرحمة . والرحيم هو الذي يرحم وليس من شروطه أن لا يفعل الرحمة. فهو تعالى رحيم لمن أراد ، وقهار لمن أراد . كما أنه تعالى نافع ضار.
Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: Mercy and generosity are only realized in our school, not in the school of the Mu'tazila. That is because generosity (al-jūd) is conferring what is appropriate, without purpose. According to the Mu'tazila, if He did not give reward and compensation (al-'iwaḍ), He would deserve blame; so by giving that reward and compensation, He frees Himself from blame; and if He is gracious, He acquires entitlement to praise from the servant. Without that graciousness, that entitlement would not be obtained. So it is established that by giving those benefits, He has acquired for Himself a benefit and compensation. Thus this is not generosity. As for our view—that nothing is obligatory upon God for anyone, and that He is deserving of praise and glory for Himself, not for a separate thing from His essence—no act of any kind of generosity and nobility has an effect in clearing Him from blame, nor an effect in obtaining increased entitlement to praise. So His gifts are pure generosity, nobility, and mercy. And the Merciful is the One who shows mercy, and it is not a condition of Him that He does not show mercy. So He, exalted, is merciful to whom He wills, and subjugator to whom He wills, just as He is beneficial and harmful.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the Sunni refutation of the Mu'tazila's conception of divine mercy and generosity. The author first defines true generosity (al-jūd) as giving what is appropriate without any ulterior motive. According to the Mu'tazila, God is obliged to reward the obedient and compensate the wronged; thus His giving is merely discharging an obligation, not pure generosity. Moreover, by giving, He acquires praise from the servant, which is a benefit to Him, making His act self-interested. In contrast, the Sunni position is that nothing is obligatory upon God; He is inherently worthy of praise regardless of His actions. Therefore, His gifts have no effect on His status—they do not remove blame nor increase praise. Consequently, His giving is pure generosity and true mercy. The author also clarifies that God being merciful does not preclude Him from being harsh; He is merciful to whom He wills and subjugator to whom He wills, just as He is both beneficial and harmful.

Key Terms
الجود
العوض
الذم
الحمد
الرحمة
القهار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Ahl al-Sunna deny God's mercy in punishing; rather they affirm both mercy and justice without obligation.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that God is merciful to whom He wills and subjugator to whom He wills, showing that mercy and severity coexist in His actions.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, why are God's gifts true generosity?
A) Because He rewards those who obey
B) Because He gives without any purpose or obligation
C) Because He is merciful to everyone
D) Because He does not punish

Key Takeaway
God's generosity is pure because it is not motivated by obligation or self-gain; He is merciful and powerful by His own will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you worship or ask of God, do you ever catch yourself thinking He 'owes' you something for your efforts? How does realizing that God's generosity is completely free, with no obligation, change the way you view your relationship with Him?
?
If God's mercy is not a response to your goodness but a pure gift, what does that mean for how you approach Him after a sin? Are you willing to accept that His forgiveness is not a right but a gratuitous act, and let that move you to deeper gratitude rather than presumption?
?
The lesson shows that true generosity must be without self-interest. Examine your own acts of giving: do you give expecting thanks, reward, or status? How does the definition of al-jūd challenge you to purify your intentions before the One who is the Source of all generosity?

The Meaning of the Name al-Rahman
The author explains that 'al-Rahman' indicates intensity in mercy, with two aspects: God's mercy is without compensation, and unlike creatures' mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intensive form 'al-Rahman' is uniquely applied to God, specifically that His mercy is without any purpose or compensation, unlike creatures' mercy.

Core Idea
God's mercy is true mercy because it seeks no reward, praise, or obligation.

The name 'al-Rahman' is one of God's most frequent names in the Qur'an. But what makes it special? In this passage, the author explains the intensity of this name by contrasting God's mercy with that of creatures. You will see that God's mercy is completely gratuitous, while all other mercy is motivated by some form of compensation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then follow with the English translation. Notice the rhythmic phrasing and the mention of two aspects.
The passage is cut off at the end, but the main point is clear.
2
Identify the Two Reasons
The passage says the intensity of 'al-Rahman' points to two aspects. List them. The first is given fully; the second starts but is incomplete.
We only have part of the second reason; we infer it relates to emotional states in creatures.
3
Reflect on Compensation
The first reason lists several types of compensation: reward, praise, obligation, and tenderness. Consider why these are all still 'compensations'.
Even emotional tenderness is a kind of compensation because it fulfills a need in the heart.
4
Understand the Contrast
Contrast God's mercy with all these. God has no need and seeks no return. Therefore His mercy is the true mercy, worthy of the name 'al-Rahman'.
This reinforces the Sunni idea of God's perfection and independence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واسم الرحمن يدل على المبالغة في الرحمة. وهذه المبالغة إشارة إلى وجهين : الأول : إن كل ما سواه فإنما يرحم لاكتساب عوض . وهو إما استحقاق الثواب ، أو استحقاق الثناء ، أو التخلص من الذمة ، أو دافع الرقة الحقيقية من القلب . وكل ذلك أعواض . فأما الحق سبحانه فإنه يرحم لا لغرض أصلاً . فكانت رحمته هي الرحمة الحقيقة . ولهذا السبب استحق أن يسمى رحماناً . الثاني : إن جميع الخلق لا يرحمون أحداً ، إلا إذا حصلت في قلوبهم
And the name 'al-Rahman' indicates intensity in mercy. This intensity points to two aspects: First: Everything other than Him only shows mercy to acquire compensation. That is either entitlement to reward, or entitlement to praise, or discharge of obligation, or the motive of genuine tenderness in the heart. And all of these are compensations. As for the Truth, exalted, He shows mercy for no purpose at all. So His mercy is true mercy. For this reason He deserves to be called 'al-Rahman'. Second: All creatures do not show mercy to anyone unless it occurs in their hearts...
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage expounds on the name 'al-Rahman', which is an intensive form indicating the utmost degree of mercy. The author gives two reasons for this intensity. The first reason: every being other than God only shows mercy to gain some compensation, such as reward, praise, discharge of an obligation, or even the emotional satisfaction of compassion. In contrast, God's mercy is entirely without purpose or compensation. Thus His mercy is the true mercy, and He alone deserves the name 'al-Rahman'. The second reason, which is cut off, likely states that creatures' mercy depends on an emotional state in their hearts, while God's mercy is not subject to such change. This passage reinforces the Sunni doctrine of divine mercy as purely gratuitous and unconstrained.

True Mercy and the Meaning of Ar-Rahman
Ahl al-Sunna define true mercy and explain the name Ar-Rahman with two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni definition of true mercy as giving without any purpose or compensation, and how the name Ar-Rahman uniquely applies to Allah.

Core Idea
True mercy is giving without needing anything in return—only Allah qualifies.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah clarifies a fundamental difference between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila regarding divine mercy. The author first defines generosity (jūd) as giving what is appropriate without ulterior motive, then refutes the Mu'tazilite view that God's giving is to avoid blame. Finally, he explains the name Ar-Rahman in two aspects, showing why Allah's mercy is the only true mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the contrast between Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the key contrast in generosity
Focus on the definition of generosity (jūd) and how the Mu'tazila's view makes God's giving a form of self-benefit, contrasting with the Sunni view that God gives purely out of generosity.
3
Understand the two aspects of 'Rahman'
The name Ar-Rahman indicates exaggeration in mercy. The first aspect is that God shows mercy without any compensation, unlike others. The second aspect is that all mercy in creation originates from God.
These two aspects highlight the exclusivity and absoluteness of divine mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلقه وتكليفه مع علمه بأن عاقبته تؤدي إلى هذه الحالة : سعياً في الإضرار والإيلام . وذلك لا يليق بالرحمة
قال أهل السنة والجماعة : الرحمة الكرم إنما تتحقق على مذهبنا ، لا على مذهب المعتزلة . وذلك لأن الجود هو إفادة ما ينبغي ، لا لغرض . وعند المعتزلة أنه تعالى لو لم يعط الثواب والعوض ، فإنه يستحق الذم ، فهو بإعطاء ذلك الثواب ، وذلك العوض ، يتخلص من الذم ، وإذا تفضل فقد اكتسب استحقاق الحمد من العبد . ولولا ذلك التفضل ، لما حصل ذلك الاستحقاق . فثبت : أنه تعالى بإعطاء تلك المنافع قد اكتسب لنفسه منفعة وعوضاً . فلم يكن هذا جوداً . فأما على قولنا : إنه لا يجب على الله لأحد شيئاً ، وأنه مستحق الثناء والحمد لذاته ، لا لأمر منفصل (۱) عن ذاته . لم يكن [ لفعل) ] شيء من أنواع الجود والكرم ، أثر في تخليته (۳) عن الذم ، ولا أثر في تحصيل مزيد استحقاق الحمد. فكانت عطاياه محض الجود والكرم والرحمة . والرحيم هو الذي يرحم وليس من شروطه أن لا يفعل الرحمة. فهو تعالى رحيم لمن أراد ، وقهار لمن أراد . كما أنه تعالى نافع ضار.
·

رحماناً .
والثاني : إن جميع الخلق لا يرحمون أحداً ، إلا إذا حصلت في قلوبهم داعية الرحمة ، والخالق لتلك الداعية هو الله سبحانه ، فكان التراحم في الحقيقة ليس إلا الله سبحانه من هذا الوجه. فهذا هو الكلام في هذا الباب
والله أعلم بالصواب
تمناء
His creation and his burdening with duties despite His knowledge that his end leads to this state: striving to harm and cause pain. And that is not befitting mercy. Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: Mercy and generosity are only realized according to our school, not according to the Mu'tazila. That is because generosity is giving what is befitting, not for a purpose. According to the Mu'tazila, if He (exalted) did not give reward and compensation, He would deserve blame. So by giving that reward and that compensation, He frees Himself from blame, and if He bestows favor, He has acquired deserving of praise from the servant. And were it not for that bestowal, that deserving would not have occurred. So it is established: that He (exalted) by giving those benefits has acquired for Himself benefit and compensation. So this was not generosity. As for according to our statement: that nothing is obligatory upon God for anyone, and that He deserves praise and laud for His Essence, not for a matter separate (1) from His Essence. No act of any type of generosity and kindness had an effect in clearing Him (3) from blame, nor an effect in attaining additional deserving of praise. So His gifts were pure generosity, kindness, and mercy. And the Merciful is He who shows mercy, and it is not among its conditions that He does not do mercy. So He (exalted) is Merciful to whom He wills, and Dominant over whom He wills. Just as He (exalted) is Beneficial, Harmful. And the name 'Ar-Rahman' indicates exaggeration in mercy. And this exaggeration points to two aspects: First: Everything other than Him only shows mercy to acquire compensation. That is either deserving reward, or deserving praise, or freeing from obligation, or the drive of genuine tenderness from the heart. And all of that is compensation. As for the Truth (exalted), He shows mercy for no purpose at all. So His mercy is the true mercy. And for this reason He deserves to be called 'Ar-Rahman'. Second: All of creation do not show mercy to anyone unless a motive of mercy occurs in their hearts, and the Creator of that motive is God (exalted). So communion in mercy in reality is only God (exalted) from this aspect. This is the discussion on this topic. And God knows best what is correct. Completed.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of true mercy (rahma) and the name Ar-Rahman, presented by Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a in contrast to the Mu'tazila. The author first establishes that generosity (jūd) is giving what is appropriate without any ulterior purpose. The Mu'tazila hold that if God did not reward and compensate, He would be blameworthy; thus His giving is a means to avoid blame and acquire praise from the servant. This makes His giving a form of self-benefit, not pure generosity. Ahl al-Sunna, however, affirm that nothing is obligatory upon God, and He is inherently worthy of praise. His acts of giving have no effect on removing blame or increasing His worthiness of praise. Therefore, His gifts are pure generosity, kindness, and mercy. The author then clarifies that 'Merciful' (al-Rahīm) is not conditioned on not being able to withhold mercy; God is merciful to whom He wills and dominant over whom He wills. The name 'Ar-Rahman' indicates an exaggeration in mercy, which is explained through two aspects. First, every other being shows mercy only to gain some compensation (like reward, praise, discharge of obligation, or emotional relief), whereas God shows mercy for absolutely no purpose, making His mercy true mercy. Second, all creatures only show mercy when a motive arises in their hearts, and that motive is created by God. Therefore, from this perspective, real mercy is only from God. The passage concludes this discussion.

جود (jūd)
كرم (karam)
رحمن (Rahman)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine mercy is like human mercy, given for some purpose or compensation.
Correction: According to Ahl al-Sunna, divine mercy is given without any purpose or compensation, making it true mercy.

Checkpoint

A) Divine mercy is given for compensation, while created mercy is free.
B) Divine mercy is given without any purpose, while created mercy is for some compensation.

Key Takeaway
Allah's mercy is the only true mercy because He gives without any need for compensation, and all mercy in creation originates from Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do a good deed, do you expect something in return—whether praise from others, a sense of self-satisfaction, or even a reward from Allah? How does that expectation compare to Allah's mercy, which gives without any purpose or compensation? What would it look like for you to give even a small act of kindness without any thought of return?
?
The lesson reveals that every created being shows mercy only to gain some compensation—even if just an emotional release. Are you aware of the hidden motives behind your own kindness? Would you still show mercy if you knew for certain that no one would ever know, no reward would come, and no emotional benefit would result? What holds you back from that level of purity?
?
Allah is both 'Merciful to whom He wills' and 'Dominant over whom He wills.' Does this truth comfort you or trouble you? Are you willing to trust that His mercy and His severity are both perfectly just and wise, even when He withholds mercy from someone you love or allows hardship in your own life? How can you cultivate that trust starting today?

The Mu'tazila Objection on Deserving Praise and the Sunni Response
Mu'tazila objection and Sunni retort beginning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila argument that only their school makes sense of praising God, and why the Sunnis reject this claim.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila claim determinism makes God unworthy of praise; Sunnis maintain the opposite.

The Mu'tazila present a forceful objection against the Sunni view of divine determinism. They argue that if God creates all actions, including evil, then He cannot be genuinely praised. This lesson presents their argument and the opening rebuttal of Ahl al-Sunna.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Begin by reading the full Mu'tazila objection. Notice how they define the criteria for deserving praise and apply it to God on their own school.
The argument uses the idea that no ugliness, injustice, or futility can be attributed to God.
2
Understand their example and dilemma
The Mu'tazila give the example of creating disbelief and punishing without compensation. Then they pose a logical dilemma: Does God deserve praise from the servant or from Himself? Both options seem problematic on determinism.
The dilemma is meant to show that only free will can make praise meaningful.
3
See the Sunni response's starting point
The Sunni response begins by reversing the claim: 'Rather this is only valid according to our statement.' The full response continues on subsequent pages, but the direction is set.
Notice that the Sunnis do not accept the Mu'tazila's premises about ugliness and justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع
قالت المعتزلة : هذا لا يتم إلا على مذهبنا . وذلك لإن المستحق للحمد هو الذي لا قبح في فعله ، ولا جود في قضيته ، ولا سوء في مشيته ، ولا عيب في صنعه. وعندنا : أن الله كذلك. فكان مستحقاً لأعظم المحامد والمدائح . أما على مذهب الجبر ، فلا قبيح إلا وهو فعله ، ولا جور إلا وهـو قضيته ، ولا عبث إلا وهو صنعه . لإنه يخلق الكفر في الكافر ثم يعذبه عليه ويؤلم الحبر امانـه (۳)] من غير أن يعوضها، فامتنع كونه مستحقاً للحمد والمدائح . وأيضاً : ذلك الحمد الذي يستحقه بسبب كونه إلها . إما أن يستحقه على العبد ، أو على نفسه . فإن استحقه على العبد ، وجب أن يكون [ العبد (۳) ] قادراً على الفعل . وذلك يبطل القول بالجبر . وإن كان يستحقه على نفسه ، كان معناه أنه يجب عليه أن يحمد نفسه . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . فثبت : أن قوله ( الحمد الله ) : لا يتم إلا على قولنا
وجوه :
قال أهل السنة والجماعة : بل هذا لا يتم إلا على قولنا . وبيانه من
Chapter Four. The Mu'tazila said: This is only valid according to our school. That is because the one deserving of praise is he whose act has no ugliness, no injustice in his decree, no evil in his will, no defect in his making. According to us, God is like that. So He deserves the greatest praises and eulogies. As for according to the school of determinism (jabr), there is no ugly act except it is His act, no injustice except it is His decree, no futility except it is His making. Because He creates disbelief in the disbeliever, then torments him for it and pains the [unclear: 'al-habr amānah'?] without compensating them. So He cannot be deserving of praise and eulogies. Also: That praise which He deserves due to His being God. Either He deserves it from the servant, or from Himself. If He deserves it from the servant, then it is necessary that the servant be capable of the act. And that invalidates the claim of determinism. And if He deserves it from Himself, then its meaning is that it is obligatory upon Him to praise Himself. And it is known that that is false. So it is established: that the statement 'Praise be to God' is only valid according to our statement. Aspects: Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a said: Rather this is only valid according to our statement. And its clarification is from...
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the Mu'tazila and the beginning of the Sunni response. The Mu'tazila argue that the statement 'Praise be to God' (al-Hamdu Lillāh) is only valid on their own theological school (madhhab). They claim that one who deserves praise must have no ugliness, injustice, evil, or defect in His actions, decree, will, and making. On their view, God fits this description. But on the deterministic view (madhhab al-jabr), every evil act, injustice, and futility is attributed to God. The Mu'tazila give the example of God creating disbelief in the disbeliever and then punishing him without compensation, which they consider unjust. Therefore, God cannot be praised on determinism. They also present a logical dilemma: the praise God deserves either is from the servant or from Himself. If from the servant, then the servant must have free will, which invalidates determinism. If from Himself, then it would obligate God to praise Himself, which is absurd. Thus they conclude that their own view is necessary for the validity of 'Praise be to God'. The Sunni response begins by asserting the opposite: that this statement is only valid on their own school. The full response is not given on this page; it continues in the following pages.

Key Terms
حمد (hamd)
جبر (jabr)
قبيح (qabīḥ)
جور (jawr)
عبث ('abath)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That determinism necessarily attributes evil to God in a way that makes Him unworthy of praise.
Correction: Sunnis respond that praiseworthiness is based on God's essence and wisdom, not on human criteria of ugliness and justice.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila in this passage, why does the deterministic view make God undeserving of praise?
A) Because God creates evil and then punishes without compensation.
B) Because God does not exist on their view.
C) Because God is not a free agent.
D) Because God does not command good.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use a logical and moral argument to challenge determinism, but Sunnis reject their premises about praise-worthiness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'alhamdulillah,' do you ever feel a tension between praising God and acknowledging that some of His decrees seem painful or unjust by your own measure? How do you resolve that in your heart?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that God's praise-worthiness depends on His actions being free from defect. Do you find yourself sometimes evaluating God's actions based on your own sense of fairness? What would it mean to praise Him regardless?
?
If you truly believe that everything is from God, including trials and evils, does that make your worship more difficult or more profound? Examine your own prayers: are you praising God only for what you see as good, or for His entire will?
