The Meaning of 'Lord of the Worlds': Mu'tazilite and Sunni Arguments
Mu'tazilite definition of tarbiyah and Sunni refutation with three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definition of tarbiyah and the Sunni response through three proofs that establish God as the creator of all actions.

Core Idea
True lordship means God creates all actions, not only benefits.

The phrase 'Lord of the Worlds' is a cornerstone of Islamic theology. But what does 'lordship' truly entail? The Mu'tazilites proposed a narrow definition focused on benefit. Sunni scholars, however, demonstrated through three powerful proofs that this verse actually supports the view that God creates every action—including human choices. Let's explore this debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite view
Read the Mu'tazilite definition of tarbiyah and their claim that everything God brings is a benefit for the purpose of beneficence. Notice how they contrast this with the Jabriyya.
Consider why they think lordship and benefit are linked.
2
Examine the first Sunni proof
The first proof argues that since the world includes all existents, human actions are included. Therefore, God as Lord must be the disposer and creator of those actions.
This directly refutes the idea that humans independently create their acts.
3
Analyze the second proof (the rope analogy)
The second proof uses the example of a person throwing a rope to someone they know will use it to hang themselves. This shows that obligation cannot be purely for benefit if God knows it leads to punishment.
Focus on how this undermines the Mu'tazilite concept of beneficence.
4
Reflect on the third proof
The third proof is a reductio ad absurdum: if faith were from the servant's own creation, then the servant's tarbiyah would be more complete than God's, making the servant more a lord than God—which is impossible.
This shows the logical necessity of God creating all actions, including faith.
5
Synthesize the Sunni position
After reading the commentary, summarize how the three proofs together establish that 'Lord of the Worlds' implies God's creation of all human actions.
Connect back to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس: تبيان (رب العالمين). قالت المعتزلة: التربية عبارة عن إيصال المنافع إلى الغير، لغرض الإحسان إليه. فلما وصف الله نفسه بأنه رب العالمين، وجب أن يقال: إن كل ما أوصل الله إلى الغير فإنه منفعة، وأنه تعالى إنما أوصلها إليهم لغرض الإحسان إليهم. وهذا إنما يتم على قولنا. فإنا نقول: إنه تعالى ما خلق الخلق، وما كلف أحداً إلا لغرض الإحسان إليهم. فأما على قول الجبرية، وهو أنه تعالى خلقهم للنار والعذاب الدائم، فهذا لا يكون رباً ولا مربياً البتة. قال أهل السنة والجماعة: هذه الآية من أدل الدلائل على صحة قولنا. وبيانه من وجوه: الأول: إن العالم كل موجود سوى الله، فتكون أفعال العباد من جملة العالم. ولما كان رباً للعالمين، وجب أن يكون رباً لأفعال العباد والرب هو المتصرف بالشيء فيلزم كونه متصرفاً في أفعال العباد، والتصرف في أفعال العباد لا يكون إلا بخلقها وتكوينها. قد دلت هذه الآية على أنه تعالى خالق لأفعال العباد. الثاني: إنه تعالى لما علم أن الكافر لا يؤمن، وأنه لا يستفيد من صيرورته مأموراً بالإيمان إلا استحقاق العذاب الدائم، فكيف يمكن أن يقال: إنه تعالى إنما كلفه لغرض الإحسان؟ ومثاله: من وقع في البئر، وعلم إنسان علماً يقيناً: أنه لو رمى إليه حبلاً، فإنه يخنق نفسه، ثم إنه مع هذا اليقين رمى إليه حبلاً، وقال: غرضي منه أن يستعين به على الصعود من البئر. فإن كل أحد يقول: إنك علمت أنه لا يصرف ذلك الحبل إلا إلى مضرة نفسه، فكان إلقاء الحبل إليه محض الإضرار. فكذا ههنا. الثالث: إن الإيمان أكثر نفعاً للعبد من كل سواه، ولما كان إيمان العبد من خلقه، كان تربية العبد لنفسه أكمل من تربية الله له. فوجب أن يكون كون العبد رباً ومربياً لنفسه، أكمل من كون الله رباً ومربياً له. وهذا يوجب أن يصح من الله التمدح بكونه رباً للعالمين. والله أعلم.
Chapter Five: Explanation of 'Lord of the Worlds'. The Mu'tazilites said: Tarbiyah is conveying benefits to others for the purpose of beneficence to him. So when God described Himself as Lord of the Worlds, it became necessary to say: Everything that God conveys to others is a benefit, and that He, exalted, only conveys them to them for the purpose of beneficence to them. And this is only completed according to our view. For we say: He, exalted, did not create creation nor obligate anyone except for the purpose of beneficence to them. But according to the view of the Jabriyya, which is that He, exalted, created them for the Fire and eternal punishment, this one is not a lord nor a nurturer at all. Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said: This verse is among the strongest evidences for the correctness of our view. Its explanation is in aspects: The first: The world is every existent other than God, so the actions of servants are part of the world. And since He is Lord of the Worlds, it is necessary that He be Lord of the actions of servants, and the Lord is the disposer of the thing, so it follows that He is a disposer of the actions of servants, and disposal of the actions of servants is only through creating and bringing them into existence. This verse has indicated that He, exalted, is the Creator of the actions of servants. The second: He, exalted, knew that the disbeliever will not believe and that he will not benefit from his being commanded to believe except by deserving eternal punishment. So how can it be said that He, exalted, only obligated him for the purpose of beneficence? Its example: One who fell into a well, and a person knew with certain knowledge that if he threw a rope to him, he would strangle himself, then despite this certainty he threw a rope to him and said: 'My purpose is that he use it to climb out of the well.' Then everyone would say: 'You knew that he would only use that rope to harm himself, so throwing the rope to him was pure harm.' So similarly here. The third: Faith is more beneficial to the servant than anything else, and since the servant's faith is from his own creation, the servant's tarbiyah of himself would be more complete than God's tarbiyah of him. So it would be necessary that the servant being a lord and nurturer of himself be more complete than God being a lord and nurturer of him. This necessitates that it be valid for God to praise Himself as Lord of the Worlds. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite argument about the meaning of 'Lord of the Worlds' and the Sunni refutation through three proofs. The Mu'tazilites define tarbiyah (nurturing/lordship) as conveying benefits for the purpose of beneficence. They argue that since God calls Himself 'Lord of the Worlds', everything He brings to others must be a benefit, and He does so to be kind. This only works if, as they claim, God only creates and obligates for beneficence. They contrast this with the Jabriyya, who hold that God creates some people for eternal punishment—which for the Mu'tazilites contradicts true lordship. The Sunnis respond that the verse actually proves their own position, and they give three proofs. The first proof: The world includes all that is not God, so human actions are part of it. Since God is Lord of the world, He must be Lord of human actions, meaning He disposes them, which requires creating them. Thus the verse proves God creates all acts. The second proof: God knows the disbeliever will not believe and command brings only punishment. So obligation cannot be for beneficence, because like throwing a rope to someone you know will use it to hang himself, it is purely harmful. The third proof: If a servant's faith came from himself, then his own nurturing of himself would be more complete than God's, implying the servant would be more lord of himself than God—an absurdity that would invalidate God's praise as Lord. Thus the verse supports the Sunni view that God creates all actions, including faith.

تربية
الجبرية
أفعال العباد

Misconception: Some think that 'Lord of the Worlds' only means God provides benefits and does not interfere with human free will.
Correction: The Sunni proofs show that lordship includes being the creator and disposer of all human actions, establishing divine predestination over all acts.

Checkpoint
According to the first Sunni proof, why does God being 'Lord of the Worlds' entail that He creates human actions?
A) Because human actions are part of the world and the Lord is the disposer of everything in it.
B) Because God must be fair in judging actions.
C) Because human actions are not real existents.
D) Because the Mu'tazilites said so.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'Lord of the Worlds' proves that God is the creator of all actions, contrary to the Mu'tazilite view that lordship only entails benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If God creates all your actions, including your sins, do you truly repent with humility, or do you find yourself blaming Him and excusing your disobedience? How would it change your prayer today to acknowledge that even your ability to repent is His creation?
?
The Mu'tazilites insisted God only intends good. When you face a trial that seems purely harmful, do you secretly doubt God's goodness? How does this lesson's teaching that God's lordship includes creating all things—even difficult ones—reshape your trust in Him right now?
?
The third proof shows that if your faith were your own doing, you would be more your own lord than God. In what areas of your life—your career, relationships, or spirituality—are you still acting as if you are the ultimate disposer? What would it look like to surrender that control today?

The Debate Over 'Master of the Day of Judgment'
Mu'tazilite argument and Sunni refutation on 'Master of the Day of Judgment'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite interpretation of 'Master of the Day of Judgment' and the Sunni refutation showing that it actually supports their doctrine of divine predestination.

Core Idea
God being Master of Judgment Day actually proves He controls all actions.

This passage presents a classic debate over the meaning of 'Master of the Day of Judgment' (مالك يوم الدين). The Mu'tazilites argue that 'din' means recompense, which only makes sense if humans have free will. The Sunnis respond with a powerful reductio ad absurdum, showing that if actions were truly up to humans, God would not be Master but worse than a slave.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite argument
Start by reading the Mu'tazilite claim that 'din' means recompense, and recompense only makes sense if there is an action. Note how they conclude that humans must have their own actions (free will).
This is a classic Mu'tazilite proof for free will from the Qur'anic verse.
2
Understand the Sunni hypothetical
Now read the Sunni response. They set up a hypothetical scenario: a man disbelieves for 80 years, then believes for a moment and dies. Ask yourself: what would this mean if the actions were truly his own?
Focus on the logic of the reductio.
3
Analyze the reductio
The Sunnis argue that on the Mu'tazilite view, such a man would be blameworthy and God would be stripped of divinity, becoming like a slave—even worse. Understand why this is absurd to the Sunnis.
The key is that God would have no freedom to release the man from obligation.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this argument supports the Sunni position that all actions are created by God, and that God is the true Master with absolute authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل: (مالك يوم الدين). قالت المعتزلة: الدين هو الجزاء. والجزاء إنما يحسن على الفعل فإذا كان لا فعل للعبد، امتنع ترتب الجزاء عليه. فوجب أن يكون للعبد فعل. قال أهل السنة والجماعة: كونه تعالى مالكاً ليوم الدين، لا يتم إلا على قولنا. وبيانه من وجوه: الأول: إنا نفرض رجلاً استمر على كفره ثمانين سنة، ثم اتفق أن آمن، ومات عقيب إيمانه بلحظة. فهذا الرجل عصى الله بالكفر في جميع عمره، ثم أتى بالإيمان لحظة واحدة، فإنه ينعزل عن الإلهية، ويصير سفيها مستحقاً للذم. وعلى هذا التقدير لا يكون مالكاً، بل يكون مملوكاً، بل يكون حاله أشد من حال مملوك، لأن المملوك قد يتوقع الخلاص عن ذل تكليف المولى. والله تعالى لا رضاء له البتة في الخلاص عن ذلك التكليف.
It was said: (Master of the Day of Judgment). The Mu'tazilites said: al-Din is recompense. And recompense is only appropriate upon action. So if the servant has no action, then the recompense cannot follow upon him. So the servant must have action. Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said: His being, exalted, Master of the Day of Judgment is only completed according to our view. Its explanation is in aspects: The first: We suppose a man who persisted in his disbelief for eighty years, then happened that he believed, and died immediately after his belief. This man disobeyed God by disbelief all his life, then brought faith for a single moment. Then he would be stripped of divinity, and become foolish deserving blame. On this assumption, he would not be a master, but rather a slave, rather his state would be more severe than the state of a slave, because the slave may hope for release from the humiliation of the master's obligation. And God, exalted, has no satisfaction at all in release from that obligation.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite argument about the verse 'Master of the Day of Judgment' and the Sunni refutation. The Mu'tazilites interpret 'din' as recompense, which only makes sense if it follows an action. Thus, if the servant had no action (i.e., all actions are from God), recompense would be impossible. So the servant must have his own actions (free will). The Sunnis respond that God being Master of the Day of Judgment is only consistent with their own view (that God creates all actions). They present a proof by reductio ad absurdum: Suppose a man disbelieves for eighty years, then believes and dies instantly. If his actions were his own, then his obedience is minimal compared to his disobedience. Yet according to Mu'tazilite reasoning, he would be deserving of blame, and God would not be Master but rather a slave—even worse than a slave, because a slave may hope for release, but God would have no pleasure in release from that obligation. This shows the absurdity of attributing actions to the servant, thus confirming that God is the true Master and Creator of all actions.

يوم الدين
الجزاء
فعل العبد
الجبرية
ملك

Checkpoint

A) The man would be rewarded for his faith.
B) God would be stripped of divinity and be in a worse state than a slave.
C) The man would be neither punished nor rewarded.
D) God would be just in punishing him.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni refutation uses a hypothetical life of disbelief and last-minute faith to show that attributing actions to humans would make God not truly Master.

The Sunni Refutation of Mu'tazila on 'Iyaka Nasta'in'
Ahl al-Sunna refutes Mu'tazila claim that seeking aid implies human independent action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Sunna uses the verse 'Iyaka nasta'in' to refute the Mu'tazila claim that seeking help implies human independent action.

Core Idea
Seeking help proves dependence, not independence.

The verse 'You alone we ask for help' seems to imply that the servant has an act to seek help for. The Mu'tazila argued this proves human independent agency. But Ahl al-Sunna turn this argument on its head, showing that the very structure of the verse proves that all power belongs to Allah. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two speakers
Read the passage and identify the two speakers: the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna. Notice how the passage begins with the Mu'tazila argument ('قالوا') and then responds ('قال أهل السنة والجماعة').
2
Understand the Mu'tazila argument
The Mu'tazila argue that seeking help (استعانة) is only conceivable if the servant is capable and has an act. They claim the explicit phrase 'and You alone we ask for help' shows the servant is the agent of the request, implying independence.
They use the example of asking for help in height and shortness to mock the Sunni view.
3
Follow the Sunni analysis of 'aid'
Ahl al-Sunna analyze the meaning of 'aid' (إعانة) in the verse. They propose two possibilities: aid to attain faith itself, or aid in performing worship. They reject the first with two reasons, and accept the second based on the verse's structure.
The first reason: faith is already obtained, so requesting it is futile. The second: that would be aid in acquiring means, not in worship.
4
Grasp the principle of two effectors
The Sunni conclude that the aid sought is for the act of worship itself, which requires Allah's power to have an effect. Since two independent effectors cannot produce one effect, the servant's power cannot have any effect, negating independence.
This is a key kalam principle: impossibility of the concurrence of two effectors on one effect (استحالة اجتماع مؤثرين على أثر واحد).
5
Reflect on the strength of the proof
Consider why Ahl al-Sunna call this verse 'among the strongest evidences'. It uses the very text the Mu'tazila relied on to prove the opposite, turning it into a refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن في (إياك نستعين) قالوا : هذا إنما يتم على قولنا : إن للعبد فعلاً وتصرفاً . لأن الاستعانة والإعانة لا يعقلان إلا في القادر على الفعل. أما إذا لم يكن له فعل ، فكيف تعقل الاستعانة ؟ وهل يجوز أن يقال : إياك أستعين في طولي وفي قصري ، وفي خلق السماء والأرض ؟ ولما لم يجز ذلك علمنا : أن الاستعانة هي طلب المعونة على ما يقدر العبد عليه ، ويحصل من جهته . وأيضاً : فقوله ( وإياك نستعين ) : نص في كون العبد فاعلا لهذا الطلب . فوجب كونه مستقلا بالفعل . قال أهل السنة والجماعة : هذه الآية من أدل الدلائل . لأن الآية تدل على طلب الإعانة وهذه الإعانة إما أن تكون عبارة عن تحصيل نفس الإيمان أو عبارة عن تحصيل الإعانة على العبادة والأول باطل ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إن كل ذلك حاصل . وطلب الحاصل عبث . الثاني : إن على هذا التقدير تكون هذه الإعانة إعانة في تحصيل القدرة بالآلة وإزالة الموانع ، لا في نفس العبادة . والآية دالة على أن المطلوب في هذا الدعاء هو تحصيل الإعانة على العبادة . لأنه ذكر العبادة أولاً في قوله : ( إياك نعبد ، وإياك نستعين ) وهذا يدل على أن المطلوب هو الاستعانة فيما تقدم ذكره . وهو العبادة . فثبت : أن المطلوب في هذه الآية هو الإعانة في نفس العبادة . ولا بد وأن تكون لقدرة الله تعالى أثر في نفس تلك العبادة ومتى كان ذلك ، امتنع أن يكون لقدرة العبد فيها أثر . لما بينا أن اجتماع المؤثرين على الأثر الواحد : محال . فعبارة هذه الآية دالة على قولنا من هذا الوجه والله أعلم
Chapter Eight on (You alone we ask for help). They said: This only holds according to our statement that the servant has an act and a capacity. For seeking help and giving help are only conceivable in one who has power over the act. But if the servant has no act, how can seeking help be conceivable? Is it permissible to say: 'You alone I seek help in my height and in my shortness, and in the creation of the heavens and the earth'? Since that is not permissible, we know that seeking help is asking for assistance in what the servant is capable of and which proceeds from him. Also, His statement (and You alone we ask for help) is explicit that the servant is an agent of this request. So it is necessary that he be independent in the act. Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah said: This verse is among the strongest evidences. Because the verse indicates seeking aid, and this aid is either an expression of attaining faith itself or an expression of attaining aid for worship. The first is invalid, and two aspects indicate it: First: All of that is already obtained, and requesting what is already obtained is futile. Second: On this assumption, this aid would be aid in acquiring ability through means and removing obstacles, not in the act of worship itself. The verse indicates that what is sought in this supplication is attaining aid for worship, because He mentioned worship first in His statement: (You alone we worship, and You alone we ask for help). This indicates that what is sought is help in what was mentioned before, namely worship. So it is established that what is sought in this verse is aid in the act of worship itself. And it is necessary that the power of Allah the Exalted have an effect in that very act of worship. When that is the case, it is impossible that the power of the servant have any effect in it, because we have established that the concurrence of two effectors on one effect is impossible. Thus, the wording of this verse indicates our doctrine from this aspect. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by Ahl al-Sunnah of a Mu'tazilite objection based on the verse 'Iyaka nasta'in' (You alone we ask for help).

First, the Mu'tazila argue that seeking help presupposes the servant has an act and capacity; otherwise seeking help would be meaningless. They claim the phrase 'And You alone we ask for help' is explicit that the servant is the agent of this request, proving he is independent in action.

Ahl al-Sunnah respond that the verse is actually one of the strongest proofs for their position. They analyze the meaning of 'aid' (i'ānah) in the verse. The aid sought must be either: (1) the acquisition of faith itself, or (2) aid in performing worship. The first option is invalid for two reasons: (a) faith is already obtained (the believer already has faith), so asking for it is futile; (b) if it were aid in acquiring faith, it would be aid in acquiring ability (qudrah) through means and removing obstacles, not in the act of worship itself. But the verse pairs 'worship' first ('You alone we worship') with 'help' ('and You alone we ask for help'), showing that the help is sought for what was mentioned first, i.e., worship. Thus the aid requested is in the very act of worship.

From this, Ahl al-Sunnah conclude that Allah's power must have an effect in the act of worship itself. If Allah's power has an effect, then it is impossible for the servant's power to also have an effect in the same act, because two independent effectors cannot produce one effect. This impossibility (social of two causes on one effect) is a kalam principle. Therefore, the verse actually supports the Ash'ari position that the servant's act is created by Allah, not independent.

Key Terms
إياك نستعين
استعانة
إعانة
مؤثرين
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that seeking help (استعانة) in the verse 'إياك نستعين' implies the servant has independent power to act, because one seeks help for what one can do.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunnah show that seeking help actually proves dependence: the aid is sought for the act of worship itself, which requires Allah's power to be effective, and the impossibility of two effectors means the servant's power has no independent effect.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunnah in this passage, what does the phrase 'إياك نستعين' ('You alone we ask for help') prove?
A) That the servant has independent power to act.
B) That the servant's power is the sole creator of the act.
C) That Allah's power has an effect in the act of worship, negating any effect from the servant's power.
D) That aid is sought only for matters beyond the servant's capacity.

Key Takeaway
The verse 'You alone we ask for help' proves that Allah's power is effective in the act of worship, thereby denying the servant any independent causal role.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Iyaka nasta'in' in your prayers, do you truly feel that every part of your worship—your standing, bowing, prostrating, even your intention—is directly from Allah? Or do you secretly believe you are the one performing and completing these actions? How would your prayer change if you internalized that you have no independent power in it?
?
The lesson argues that seeking help proves dependence, not independence. Have you ever felt a sense of self-satisfaction or pride in your worship, thinking 'I am a good Muslim' or 'I have accomplished this much'? How does this verse expose that pride as a form of shirk in intention?
?
If every act of obedience is a gift from Allah, then even your turning away from sin is His act upon you. In what areas of your life do you still feel self-sufficient—thinking you can overcome a bad habit or increase in good deeds by your own will? What would it look like to surrender that illusion and cry out to Allah for help in every moment?

Refuting the Objection: God's Creation of Sin Does Not Imply Willing It
Author refutes the objection that God's creation of sin entails His willing it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the objection that God's creating sin implies His willing it, and how the author refutes it by appealing to divine hatred of evil.

Core Idea
Creation does not imply approval; God may create what He hates.

This passage tackles a subtle theological objection: if God creates sinful actions, doesn't that mean He wants them? The author presents the argument, showing its entailment from the determinists' own premises, then refutes it with a clear verse. We'll walk through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening sentence presenting the opponents' argument: if God creates sins, then He wills them; but willing evil is false, so creating evil is false.
Note that the opponents are not named but are likely the Mu'tazila.
2
Examine the entailment (mulāzama)
The author explains why the Jabriya (determinists) would agree that creation implies will. This is a key step: the author uses their own logic against them.
3
See the two proofs for the entailment
The author gives two reasons: (1) the Jabriya's stance on God's will regarding all beings, and (2) the argument from specification (takhṣīṣ). Read these carefully.
4
The refutation: God hates evil
The author then presents the counter-evidence: the Quranic verse that evil is hateful to God. Since hate and will are opposites, God cannot will what He hates.
5
Connect to broader context
The author promises a fuller discussion later. For now, understand how this refutation relies on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السادس من وجهه القوم أن قالوا : لو كان الله تعالى موجداً للكفر والمعاصي ، لكان مريداً لها وهذا باطل فذاك باطل . بيان الملازمة : إن المجبرة ساعدوا على أنه تعالى لو كان خالقاً لأعمال العباد ، لكان مريداً لها. وبيان هذه المساعدة من وجهين : الأول : إنهم في مسألة إرادة الكائنات قالوا : لما ثبت أن الله خلق أعمال العباد ، وجب أن يكون مريدا لها . والثاني : أنهم قالوا إنه تعالى لما خصص بعض أفعاله ، بإحداثه في وقت معين ، ومحل معين ، مع جواز أن يخلقه في وقت آخر ، وفي محل آخر ، وجب أن يكون ذلك التخصيص لأجل الإرادة . فثبت : أنه تعالى لو كان موجداً لأفعال العباد ، لكان مريداً لها . وبيان أنه تعالى غير مريد لها : قوله تعالى : ( كل ذلك كان سيئه عند ربك مكروها ) والمكروه لا يكون مراداً لامتناع الجمع بين الضدين. وتمام الكلام في هذا المقام ، والاستدلال بجميع الآيات التي يتمسكون بها في أنه تعالى غير مريد للقبائح سيأتي الاستقصاء فيه في مسألة إرادة الكائنات إن شاء الله
The sixth type from the argument of the people: that they said: If God, exalted be He, is the bringer into existence of disbelief and sins, then He would be willing them, and this is false, so that is false. Explanation of the entailment: The Jabriya (determinists) agreed that if He, exalted be He, were the creator of the deeds of servants, then He would be willing them. And the explanation of this agreement is from two aspects: The first: That they, in the issue of the willing of beings, said: When it is established that God created the deeds of servants, it is necessary that He be willing them. And the second: That they said: When He, exalted be He, specifies some of His acts, by bringing it into existence at a specific time and a specific place, despite the permissibility that He create it at another time and another place, then that specification must be for the sake of will. So it is established: that if He, exalted be He, were the bringer into existence of the deeds of servants, then He would be willing them. And the proof that He, exalted be He, is not willing them: His saying, exalted be He: (All that is evil is hateful to your Lord) and what is hated cannot be willed, due to the impossibility of combining two opposites. And the complete discussion on this matter, and the argumentation by all the verses which they cling to, that He, exalted be He, is not willing evil acts, the thorough investigation of it will come in the issue of the willing of beings, God willing.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. It begins by presenting the sixth argument of the opponents (the Mu'tazila or similar): if God were the creator of disbelief and sins, then He would be willing them; but God willing evil is false, so the premise that He creates evil is false. The author then explains the entailment (mulāzama): the Jabriya (determinists) themselves agree that if God creates actions, He must will them. The author supports this agreement with two proofs. First, in the discussion of God's will regarding created things, the Jabriya argue that since God created actions, He necessarily wills them. Second, they argue that when God specifies an action to a particular time and place, despite the possibility of it being otherwise, that specification requires a will. Thus, if God creates actions, He wills them. Having established the entailment, the author then refutes the conclusion that God wills evil by citing the Qur'anic verse 'All that is evil is hateful to your Lord.' What is hated cannot be willed, because will and hate are opposites and cannot coexist. Therefore, since God hates evil, He does not will it, and consequently He does not create it. The author notes that a full discussion of the relevant verses will follow later in the section on God's will.

Key Terms
موجد
إرادة
مكروه
ملازمة
مجبرة
تخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates sinful actions, He must will and approve of them.
Correction: God may create what He hates without willing it as an act of obedience; the Quran affirms that He hates evil.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author use to prove that God does not will evil deeds?
A) The Jabriya's admission that God creates actions.
B) The Qur'anic verse that evil deeds are hateful to God.
C) The impossibility of God creating evil.
D) The logical contradiction between will and hate.

Key Takeaway
God's creation of sinful actions does not imply that He wills them, because He hates evil and cannot will what He hates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God does not create sin, then where does the sin in your life come from? How does this lesson challenge you to take full responsibility for your actions instead of blaming divine decree?
?
The verse 'All that is evil is hateful to your Lord' is used as proof that God does not will sin. When you sin, do you truly feel that God hates that act? How does that realization affect your remorse and commitment to change?
?
The argument hinges on the impossibility of God willing what He hates. Is there any sin in your life that you have grown comfortable with, forgetting that God hates it? What will you do today to align your will with what God loves rather than what He hates?

Refuting the Blame on God for Creating Disbelief
Sanusi defends Ash'ari position on divine creation of actions using qudra wa da'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari principle of power-plus-motive (qudra wa da'i) resolves the objection that God would be blameworthy for creating evil actions in humans.

Core Idea
God's creation of disbelief is not blameworthy because His power and will necessitate the act.

This lesson presents a classic objection against the Ash'ari view that God creates all actions, including disbelief, and then holds humans accountable. The objector argues that if God created the evil saying in people, then He would be the one truly guilty of futility. Sanusi responds by invoking the principle that in God's case, power and motive necessarily produce the act, removing any analogy with human agents. Additionally, he refers to twelve arguments that establish the obligation of the impossible (taklif ma la yutaq), further refuting the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the objector uses a Qur'anic verse (2:44) and an analogy to make his point.
2
Identify the core of the objection
The objector claims that if God created the false saying about Him having a son, then futility (عبث) is more incumbent on the creator. Understand why this is a serious challenge to divine justice.
The analogy with a human speaker who admits to creating evil highlights the perceived absurdity.
3
Examine the Ash'ari answer: qudra wa da'i
Sanusi states that with us (the Ash'aris), the combination of power and motive necessitates the act. This means God's actions are necessitated by His own attributes, not by external factors. Therefore, the objection's analogy with human agents fails.
The phrase 'مجموع القدرة والدّاعي' is key.
4
Consider the reference to the twelve aspects
Sanusi mentions twelve aspects (from earlier discussion) that demonstrate the occurrence of taklif ma la yutaq (obligation of the impossible). This further undermines the objector's position.
This part is brief; the details of the twelve aspects are not given here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال تعالى : ( أتأمرون الناس بالبر ، وتنسون أنفسكم )؟ فلما عاب عليهم قولهم إن ولداً للرحمن فالذي خلق ذلك القول فيهم يكون العبث عليه الزم. لو أن قائلاً قال : تأملوا قبح قولهم إن للرحمن ولداً، ومن قبحه انفطرت السموات وانشقت الأرض فانهدت الجبال ثم مع ذلك فإني أنا الذي فعلت ذلك، وخلقته فيهم وقضيته عليهم وأردته منهم. لقال الناس : ما أشد حماقة هذا القائل، وما أقبح صنعه. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك، فكيف يليق هذا برب العالمين؟ والجواب : إن عندنا مجموع القدرة والدّاعي، موجب للفعل، وعلى هذا التقدير فهذه الإلزامات ساقطة عنا. وأيضا : فالوجوه الاثني عشر المذكورة دالة على وقوع تكليف ما لا يطاق، وكل ذلك يبطل هذه الكلمات. والله أعلم
He said, the Exalted: 'Do you command people to righteousness and forget yourselves?' So when He criticized them for their saying that there is a child for the Most Merciful, then the one who created that saying in them, futility would be more incumbent upon him. If a speaker said: 'Consider the ugliness of their saying that the Most Merciful has a child, and from its ugliness the heavens are split and the earth is rent and the mountains collapse, then despite that, I am the one who did that, created it in them, decreed it upon them, and willed it from them.' People would say: 'How severe is the foolishness of this speaker, and how ugly his deed.' And if this is the case, how is this befitting of the Lord of the worlds? The answer: With us, the combination of power and the motive necessitates the act. On this estimation, these objections fall away from us. Also: The twelve aspects mentioned indicate the occurrence of obligation of what is not possible, and all that invalidates these words. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer from a kalam discussion about divine creation of human actions, specifically the problem of God creating disbelief and then blaming the disbeliever. The author first quotes the Qur'anic verse (2:44) to establish that God criticized people for commanding righteousness while forgetting themselves. Then the author reframes the objection: if God created the saying that He has a son in some people, then the one who created that saying would be more deserving of blame for futility. The objector analogizes: if a person claimed 'I did all the ugly things (heavens splitting, etc.) and also created the evil saying', people would call him foolish. So how can God, who creates everything including disbelief, be free from blame? The answer is the Ash'ari principle that the combination of power (qudra) and motive (da'i) necessarily results in the act. In God's case, His power and His will (the motive) necessitate the act, so His actions are not comparable to human actions which are subject to external constraints. Therefore, the objection falls away. Additionally, the author refers to twelve aspects (likely from earlier discussion) that demonstrate that obligation of what is impossible (taklif ma la yutaq) actually occurs, which further invalidates the opponent's words.

Key Terms
القدرة والداعي (qudra wa da'i)
تكليف ما لا يطاق (taklif ma la yutaq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection assumes that if God creates evil actions, He is blameworthy just as a human would be.
Correction: God's actions are necessitated by His own power and will (qudra wa da'i), not by external factors, so the human analogy does not apply.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in this passage, what combination necessitates the act and thus removes blame from God?
A) God's power alone
B) The combination of power and motive
C) The act of the human agent
D) The twelve aspects

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari principle of power-plus-motive (qudra wa da'i) provides a defense against the charge of futility in God's creation of evil actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever find yourself blaming your circumstances or even questioning God's wisdom? How does this lesson's teaching that God's power and motive necessitate His acts challenge you to take full responsibility for your actions while trusting His perfection?
?
The objector assumed God's creation of disbelief is futile. Have you ever doubted God's justice when seeing evil or disbelief in the world? How does the concept of qudra wa da'i invite you to surrender your own limited understanding and trust that God's actions are necessitated by His perfect attributes?
?
The lesson mentions 'obligation of what is not possible' (taklif ma la yutaq). Think of a moment when you faced a command from God that felt beyond your strength. Did you rely on your own power or on the One who empowers? How can this truth reshape your daily dependence on God?

First Proof: God's Handiwork Is Good
First Quranic proof that God's acts are good

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how two Quranic verses establish that everything God makes is good, and why evil cannot be attributed to Him.

Core Idea
God's perfect handiwork excludes all evil.

The author begins the discussion on divine values by citing two verses that declare God's creation to be perfect and good. From these verses, he draws a logical inference: if God's handiwork is exclusively good, then anything that is not good cannot come from God. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and its implications for understanding the origin of evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the two cited verses
Read the Arabic of the two verses: 'صنع الله الذي أتقن كل شيء' and 'الذي أحسن كل شيء خلقه'. Pay attention to the words 'أتقن' (perfected) and 'أحسن' (made good).
These verses are from Surah an-Naml (27:88) and Surah as-Sajdah (32:7).
2
Understand the logical inference
The author says: These verses show that everything that is God's handiwork is good. Therefore, anything that is not good cannot be God's handiwork. Follow the 'if-then' structure here.
3
Apply to evil and obscenities
Ugly deeds and obscenities are not described as good, so they cannot be acts of God. This is the key conclusion of the proof.
This refutes the idea that God creates both good and evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع العاشر القيم
الآيات الدالة على أن أفعاله حسنة، ومنزهة عن التفاوت والعبث والباطل فإحداها : قوله : ( صنع الله الذي أتقن كل شيء (۱) ) - ( الذي أحسن كل شيء خلقه (۲) ) فهاتان (۳) الآيتان دالتان على أن كل ما كان صنعاً الله ، فهو حسن . وهذا يقتضي أن لا يكون حسناً ، لم يكن صنعاً الله تعالى فالقبائح والفواحش لما لم تكن موصوفة بالحسن . وجب أن لا تكون فعلا الله تعالى .
وثانيها : قوله تعالى : ( وما خلقنا السموات والأرض وما بينهما باطلاً ذلك ظن الذين كفروا (٤) ) فنص على أن من اعتقد أنه تعالى خلق باطلا ، فقد كفر
وثالثها : قوله تعالى : ( ربنا ما خلقت هذا باطلا (ه) ) دل هذا الكلام على تنزيه الله تعالى عن خلق الباطل، فوجب أن يقال : إن كل ما كان باطلاً ، فإنه لا يكون مخلوقاً .
The tenth type, the values. The verses indicating that His actions are good and free from inequality, frivolity, and falsehood. The first of them: His saying: (The handiwork of Allah who has perfected everything (1)) - (Who has given everything its creation (2)). These two (3) verses indicate that everything that is the handiwork of Allah is good. This requires that whatever is not good is not the handiwork of Allah, the Exalted. So ugly deeds and obscenities, since they are not described as good, must not be an act of Allah, the Exalted. The second of them: His saying, the Exalted: (And We did not create the heavens and the earth and whatever is between them in vain; that is the opinion of those who disbelieve (4)). So He stated that whoever believes that He, the Exalted, created in vain has indeed disbelieved. The third of them: His saying, the Exalted: (Our Lord, You did not create this in vain (5)). This speech indicates the transcendence of Allah, the Exalted, above creating falsehood. So it must be said: Indeed, everything that is false is not created.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents three Quranic proofs (adillah) that God's actions are good (ḥasan) and free from imperfection, frivolity, and falsehood (bāṭil). The author first announces the topic as 'the tenth type, the values' (al-nawʿ al-ʿāshir al-qiyam), indicating a classification of proofs regarding divine acts. The first proof cites two verses: 'The handiwork of Allah who has perfected everything' (Sūrat al-Naml 27:88) and 'Who has given everything its creation' (Sūrat al-Sajdah 32:7). The author argues that these verses show that everything that is God's handiwork (ṣunʿ Allāh) is good. Therefore, anything not good cannot be God's handiwork. Consequently, ugly deeds and obscenities (al-qabāʾiḥ wa-l-fawāḥish) — since they are not described as good — must not be acts of God. The second proof quotes 'And We did not create the heavens and the earth and whatever is between them in vain' (Sūrat Ṣād 38:27), noting that the verse explicitly declares that believing God created in vain is disbelief (kufr). The third proof cites the supplication 'Our Lord, You did not create this in vain' (Sūrat Āl ʿImrān 3:191), concluding that this verse establishes God's transcendence (tanzīh) above creating falsehood, so everything false cannot be created by God. Taken together, the three proofs form a cumulative argument: God's acts are exclusively good, and evil or falsehood cannot be attributed to Him as His creation.

Key Terms
حسن
صنع الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God creates both good and evil equally.
Correction: The verses show that God's handiwork is exclusively good; evil cannot be His act.

Checkpoint
What logical conclusion does the author draw from the first two verses?
A) That God creates both good and evil.
B) That only what is made by God is good, so evil cannot be from God.
C) That God creates everything including evil.
D) That these verses are not relevant to the discussion.

Key Takeaway
God's acts are perfectly good, so evil cannot be attributed to Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face hardship or witness tragedy, do you ever feel that God has wronged you? Based on this lesson, how must you realign your heart to see that any pain is not from His handiwork? Will you today trust that His handiwork is only good, even when you cannot see it?
?
Have you ever blamed your sinful tendencies on your nature or upbringing, as if God created you that way? The lesson teaches that ugly deeds are not from God. Will you take full responsibility for your actions and seek His help to change, knowing He only creates good?
?
If you truly believe that everything God does is good, why do you still complain about your circumstances? What would change in your daily life if you internalized that every event from Allah is perfect? Commit to praising Him in every situation for the next week.

Second Proof: Creation Not in Vain
Second Quranic proof that creation is not in vain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will see how verse 38:27 refutes the belief that God created the universe in vain, thereby establishing that evil cannot be part of God's creation.

Core Idea
Believing God created in vain is disbelief.

The second proof shifts to a direct Quranic statement about the purposefulness of creation. The verse declares that God did not create the heavens and earth in vain, and labeling such an opinion as disbelief. This lesson explores how this verse reinforces the idea that God's acts are good and meaningful.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse
Read the Arabic: 'وما خلقنا السموات والأرض وما بينهما باطلاً ذلك ظن الذين كفروا'. Notice the word 'باطلاً' (in vain/falsehood).
This is from Surah Sad (38:27).
2
Understand the implication
The verse denies that creation is vain (باطل). Since evil is often considered vain or purposeless, this verse implies that evil is not part of God's creation.
The author emphasizes that believing otherwise is kufr (disbelief).
3
Connect to the overall argument
This proof complements the first: since God's creation is not in vain, everything He creates has purpose and goodness.

Third Proof: God's Transcendence Above Falsehood
Third Quranic proof that God does not create falsehood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the supplication in verse 3:191 confirms that God is free from creating falsehood, and how this completes the proof that evil is not created by God.

Core Idea
God is transcendent; He does not create falsehood.

The third proof comes from a verse where the believers praise God, declaring that He did not create anything in vain. The author argues that this statement implies God's transcendence above falsehood, so everything false cannot be created by Him. This lesson ties together the theme that God's acts are exclusively good.

Guided Path
1
Read the supplication
Read the Arabic: 'ربنا ما خلقت هذا باطلا'. Notice the address 'ربنا' (Our Lord) and the negation 'ما خلقت' (You did not create).
This is from Surah Al Imran (3:191).
2
Recognize the logical implication
The author says this speech indicates Allah's transcendence (تنزيه) above creating falsehood. Therefore, anything false (باطل) is not created by Him.
This is similar to the first proof's structure.
3
Synthesize all three proofs
Each proof eliminates falsehood, inequality, and frivolity from God's actions. Together they form a strong argument that evil is not from God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع العاشر القيم
الآيات الدالة على أن أفعاله حسنة، ومنزهة عن التفاوت والعبث والباطل فإحداها : قوله : ( صنع الله الذي أتقن كل شيء (۱) ) - ( الذي أحسن كل شيء خلقه (۲) ) فهاتان (۳) الآيتان دالتان على أن كل ما كان صنعاً الله ، فهو حسن . وهذا يقتضي أن لا يكون حسناً ، لم يكن صنعاً الله تعالى فالقبائح والفواحش لما لم تكن موصوفة بالحسن . وجب أن لا تكون فعلا الله تعالى .
وثانيها : قوله تعالى : ( وما خلقنا السموات والأرض وما بينهما باطلاً ذلك ظن الذين كفروا (٤) ) فنص على أن من اعتقد أنه تعالى خلق باطلا ، فقد كفر
وثالثها : قوله تعالى : ( ربنا ما خلقت هذا باطلا (ه) ) دل هذا الكلام على تنزيه الله تعالى عن خلق الباطل، فوجب أن يقال : إن كل ما كان باطلاً ، فإنه لا يكون مخلوقاً .
The tenth type, the values. The verses indicating that His actions are good and free from inequality, frivolity, and falsehood. The first of them: His saying: (The handiwork of Allah who has perfected everything (1)) - (Who has given everything its creation (2)). These two (3) verses indicate that everything that is the handiwork of Allah is good. This requires that whatever is not good is not the handiwork of Allah, the Exalted. So ugly deeds and obscenities, since they are not described as good, must not be an act of Allah, the Exalted. The second of them: His saying, the Exalted: (And We did not create the heavens and the earth and whatever is between them in vain; that is the opinion of those who disbelieve (4)). So He stated that whoever believes that He, the Exalted, created in vain has indeed disbelieved. The third of them: His saying, the Exalted: (Our Lord, You did not create this in vain (5)). This speech indicates the transcendence of Allah, the Exalted, above creating falsehood. So it must be said: Indeed, everything that is false is not created.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents three Quranic proofs (adillah) that God's actions are good (ḥasan) and free from imperfection, frivolity, and falsehood (bāṭil). The author first announces the topic as 'the tenth type, the values' (al-nawʿ al-ʿāshir al-qiyam), indicating a classification of proofs regarding divine acts. The first proof cites two verses: 'The handiwork of Allah who has perfected everything' (Sūrat al-Naml 27:88) and 'Who has given everything its creation' (Sūrat al-Sajdah 32:7). The author argues that these verses show that everything that is God's handiwork (ṣunʿ Allāh) is good. Therefore, anything not good cannot be God's handiwork. Consequently, ugly deeds and obscenities (al-qabāʾiḥ wa-l-fawāḥish) — since they are not described as good — must not be acts of God. The second proof quotes 'And We did not create the heavens and the earth and whatever is between them in vain' (Sūrat Ṣād 38:27), noting that the verse explicitly declares that believing God created in vain is disbelief (kufr). The third proof cites the supplication 'Our Lord, You did not create this in vain' (Sūrat Āl ʿImrān 3:191), concluding that this verse establishes God's transcendence (tanzīh) above creating falsehood, so everything false cannot be created by God. Taken together, the three proofs form a cumulative argument: God's acts are exclusively good, and evil or falsehood cannot be attributed to Him as His creation.

Key Terms
باطل
تنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) That God created falsehood as a test.
B) That everything false is not created by God.
C) That the verse is just a prayer, not a proof.
D) That falsehood is created but not by God.

Key Takeaway
God is transcendent; He does not create falsehood, so evil is not His creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own life, where have you been creating or perpetuating falsehood—lies, excuses, or deceits—while believing it is somehow from Allah? Will you repent now and stop attributing falsehood to Him?
?
If everything false is not created by Allah, then every injustice, every gossip, every broken promise originates from you. How does this truth shift your responsibility for your actions today?
?
Are there areas where you blame Allah for bad outcomes, ignoring that falsehood comes from your own choices? Will you now take full accountability and seek His forgiveness for the falsehood you have created?

Proof from Qur'anic Challenge and Objection on Human Creative Power
Presents an objection and answer about the implication of the Qur'anic challenge for human creative power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic challenge is used to argue for human creative power and how the Ash'ari school answers this objection.

Core Idea
The Qur'anic challenge implies human ability, but not independent creation.

In this passage, the author presents a key objection against the Ash'ari position on human action. The Qur'an challenges people to produce a surah like it, which seems to require that humans have genuine creative power. If they do not, the challenge would be meaningless. The Ash'ari answer defends the position that human power combined with motive can produce actions, without requiring ex nihilo creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read carefully the Arabic text and its English rendering. Notice the structure: citation, inference, objection, answer.
2
Understand the inference from the challenge
The author explains that a challenge is meaningful only if the challenged person has some ability. The inability to produce a surah despite general ability indicates a special quality of the Qur'an.
This sets up the objection.
3
Examine the objection
The objection argues that if humans cannot originate or create at all, then the challenge fails. But since the miracle is established, humans must be creators.
4
Analyze the Ash'ari answer
The answer concedes that power with motive can produce some actions. The difference in ease comes from a special quality in the act, not from lack of human power.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this argument fits into the kasb theory of acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني عشر القوم

الآيات الدالة على التحدي بالقرآن

قال تعالى : ( وإن كنتم في ريب مما نزلنا على عبدنا فأتوا بسورة من مثله ) إلى قوله : ( فإن لم تفعلوا ولن تفعلوا فاتقوا النار التي وقودها الناس والحجارة )

ووجه الاستدلال به : إن التحدي مع من يقدر على شيء من الأفعال . لأنه لما كان قادراً على الفعل في الجملة ، ثم عجز عن فعل له معنى خاص . دل ذلك على أن لذلك الفعل خصوصية. لأنه لو عجز ذلك الفاعل عن الإتيان بمسألة ، ولا يقدر على الفعل أصلا فإن التحدي معه لا يفيد الفائدة المطلوبة . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إذا لم يكن العبد قادراً على الإيجاد والتكوين أصلاً ، لم يحصل التحدي بالقرآن على كونه معجزاً . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً بإجماع الأمة ، علمنا : أن العبد موجد .

والجواب : إن هذا الإشكال غير وارد علينا . لانا سلمنا أن مجموع القدرة مع الداعي قد يوجب بعض الأفعال . وإذا لم يؤثرا في أمر من الأمور ، علمنا : أن ذلك المتيسر ممتاز عن ذلك المتعسر لمزيد خاصية ، لأجلها حصل ذلك اليسر .
The twelfth type, the people. The verses indicating the challenge with the Qur'an. He said Most High: 'And if you are in doubt about what We have sent down upon Our servant, then bring a surah like it' until His saying: 'But if you do not – and you will never do – then fear the Fire whose fuel is people and stones.' And the way of inference by it: that the challenge is with one who is able to do something of actions. Because when he is able to act in general, then becomes incapable of an act with a specific meaning, that indicates that that act has a particularity. Because if that doer were incapable of performing a matter, and he cannot do the act at all, then the challenge with him does not yield the intended benefit. If this is established, we say: If the servant is not capable of origination and creation at all, then the challenge with the Qur'an as being a miracle would not occur. And since that is false by consensus of the community, we know that the servant is a creator. And the answer: This objection is not directed at us. Because we concede that the combination of power with motive may necessitate some actions. And if they do not affect any matter, we know that that which is easy is distinguished from that which is difficult due to an additional particularity, because of which that ease occurred.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer within a kalam discussion on human creative power. First, the author cites the Qur'anic challenge verses (Surah al-Baqarah 2:23-24). The inference is explained: a challenge is only meaningful when directed at someone capable of the action. If a person is generally able but fails at a specific act, that act has a special quality. If the person had no ability at all, the challenge would be pointless. From this, an objection is raised: if humans are not capable of origination (ijad) and creation (takwin) whatsoever, then the Qur'anic challenge would not be a miracle. But since the miracle is established, it follows that the servant (al-`abd) is a creator (mujid). The answer refutes this objection for the Ash'ari position: the Ash'aris hold that the combination of power (qudra) with motive (da`i) does produce some actions. Therefore, the difference between an easy action and a difficult one arises from a special quality (khassiya) in the act, not from the lack of human power. Thus, the challenge remains valid without requiring that humans create ex nihilo.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument proves that humans create independently of God.
Correction: The Ash'ari answer demonstrates that human action is a combination of power and motive, not independent creation ex nihilo. It supports the theory of kasb (acquisition).

Checkpoint
According to the objection in the passage, what would be the consequence if the servant is not capable of origination and creation at all?
A) The Qur'an would not be a miracle.
B) The challenge would be with someone who can do everything.
C) The servant would be equal to God.
D) The servant would not be responsible.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic challenge does not prove independent human creation; rather, it is compatible with human kasb as combination of power and motive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform any action—speaking, writing, even thinking—do you truly sense that your power is not independent but conjoined with a divine motive? What would it take for you to live each moment acknowledging that your every ability is a borrowed gift from Allah?
?
The Qur'anic challenge confronts you with a choice: either admit that the Qur'an is from God or attempt to produce its like. Have you ever let the weight of this challenge sink into your heart, moving you from passive belief to active submission? If not, what is holding you back?
?
Consider a time when you felt self-sufficient or proud of your own achievements. In light of this lesson's insistence that even the 'ease' of ordinary speech comes from Allah, how can you reorient your gratitude so that you constantly attribute every success—big or small—to Him alone?

Objection from Quranic Verses on Divine Purpose and the Response
Objection from Quranic verses on divine purpose and response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that divine creation of disbelief contradicts Quranic statements about God's purpose for obedience, and the response that an affirmative proposition suffices for action.

Core Idea
God's purpose for obedience is valid even if He creates disbelief; the affirmative command stands.

This passage presents a theological objection that seems to pit God's absolute power against His revealed purposes. The objector quotes several Quranic verses to argue that if God creates disbelief, He cannot genuinely desire obedience. The author then offers a concise response, appealing to the nature of affirmative divine commands. As we read, consider how the logical structure of divine commands might resolve this apparent tension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection's structure
Read the objection carefully. Notice that it uses Quranic verses to claim that God's purpose in sending prophets is undermined if He also creates disbelief. Identify the logical connection between the verses and the conclusion.
The objector frames it as an impossibility: God cannot both create disbelief and desire obedience.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
The objector cites multiple verses: 4:94, 51:56, and the phrase 'that they might become righteous' and 'perhaps he may be reminded or fear.' Reflect on how these verses imply that God wants certain actions from humans.
These verses are crucial because they appear to ascribe purpose and desire to God regarding human actions.
3
Analyze the response's core argument
The author answers with a logical point about 'affirmative propositions' (al-qadiyya al-mujiba). Understand that an affirmative command is valid for action even if the action is not self-produced. The response also mentions God making everyone a believer 'in the value.'
This defense preserves both divine sovereignty and the seriousness of the command.
4
Consider the objection's counter-move
The objector had a further point: if obedience is created by God, the verse should say 'perhaps I would be reminded and fear' (referring to God). Notice how the response avoids this by focusing on the nature of the command.
This highlights the debate about God's speech and whether it implies possibility on His part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنوع الرابع علم اللقيم
قوله تعالى : ( وما أرسلنا من رسول إلا ليطاع بإذن الله )
دلت الآية على أن مقصوده تعالى من بعثة الأنبياء إقدام الخلق على الطاعة . فلو كان تعالى يخلق الكفر فيهم ، لامتنع أن يكون غرضه من البعثة حصول الطاعة . وهذا التمسك بقوله تعالى : ( وما خلقت الجن والإنس إلا ليعبدون ) وهكذا الاستدلال بكل ما جاء في القرآن من قوله : ( لعلهم يتقون ) - ( فقولا له : قولاً ليناً . لعله يتذكر أو يخشى ) فإن هذه النصوص وما يشبهها نصوص صريحة في أنه تعالى يريد [ من عباده فعل(*) ] الطاعة والتقوى [ ولو كانت الطاعة والتقوى ] من خلق الله تعالى لكان يجب أن يقول : لعلي أتذكر وأتقي
والجواب : إن القضية الموجبة يكفي في العمل بها ، حصول مستمر من وقت واحد وعندنا : أنه تعالى يجعل الكل مؤمناً عارفاً في القيمة
The fourth type: 'Ilm al-Laqim'. His statement, Most High: 'And We did not send any messenger except to be obeyed by permission of Allah.' The verse indicates that His purpose, Most High, from sending the prophets is the creatures' undertaking of obedience. For if He, Most High, were to create disbelief in them, it would be impossible that His purpose from the sending should be the occurrence of obedience. And this is the holding fast to His statement, Most High: 'And I did not create jinn and mankind except to worship Me.' And similarly the inference from every [verse] that has come in the Quran from His statement: 'that they might become righteous' — 'And speak to him with gentle speech that perhaps he may be reminded or fear Him.' For these texts and what resembles them are explicit texts that He, Most High, wants [from His servants the act of] obedience and piety. [And if obedience and piety were from the creation of Allah, Most High, He would have to say: 'Perhaps I would be reminded and fear.'] And the answer: The affirmative proposition (al-qadiyya al-mujiba) suffices for acting upon it; obtaining continuity from one time. And with us: that He, Most High, makes everyone a believer, knowing, in the value.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. It begins by quoting Quran 4:94, which states that messengers are sent to be obeyed. The objector infers that the purpose of prophethood is to elicit obedience from people. If God Himself were to create disbelief in them, then the purpose would be impossible—since the very obedience He aims for would be prevented by His own act. The objector then supports this with other verses, such as Quran 51:56 (creation for worship), and verses that express hope for righteousness or fear (e.g., Quran 6:51, 20:44). The claim is that these texts plainly show God wants obedience and piety from His servants. The objector adds that if obedience and piety were actually created by God, the verses would have said 'perhaps I would be reminded and fear'—meaning God would be the one acting, not the servant. The answer, though brief, argues that an affirmative proposition (like 'God wants obedience') is sufficient for acting upon it; it does not require that the obedience be self-generated. The phrase 'obtaining continuity from one time' likely means that the intention of obedience is established at a moment, and that suffices. The answer concludes by asserting that God makes everyone a believer and knowing 'in the value'—possibly referring to the afterlife or to the inherent worth of obedience. The response seems to defend the orthodox Ash'ari position that human acts are created by God yet the divine purpose remains valid.

بعثة الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God creates disbelief in a person, then God cannot genuinely desire obedience from that person.
Correction: The response shows that an affirmative command (e.g., 'obey') remains valid as a divine intention and a basis for reward or punishment, even if the act is created by God. The command itself is what matters, not the origin of the act.

Checkpoint

A) That God's purpose in sending prophets is to create disbelief.
B) That if God creates disbelief in people, His purpose of obedience would be impossible.
C) That the Quranic verses are not explicit about divine will.
D) That God's answer to the objection is that He makes everyone a believer immediately.

Key Takeaway
Divine commands and purposes remain valid even if God is the ultimate creator of human actions, because an affirmative proposition does not require independent human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever feel that God's command to obey was somehow invalid because you failed? Reflect on the truth that God's affirmative command stands regardless of your actions. How will this change your attitude toward repentance?
?
If God's purpose is fulfilled in the very command itself, not in your obedience, what does that imply about your responsibility? Does this free you from guilt or magnify your accountability? Be honest.
?
The objector assumed God's purpose must be realized universally. Have you ever doubted God's wisdom when you see widespread disbelief? How does this lesson's response help you trust in His decree while still feeling the urgency to call others to faith?

Refuting the Objection Against Divine Mercy from Creating Disbelievers
Objection-answer unit on divine mercy and punishment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the argument that God's mercy is incompatible with creating disbelievers and punishing them eternally, using scriptural counterarguments and theological reasoning.

Core Idea
God's mercy is not negated by His creating some for punishment; scripture affirms both.

This passage presents a powerful objection from the opponents against the Ash'ari view that God creates all actions, including disbelief, and punishes the disbeliever eternally. The author not only states the objection clearly but also provides a concise refutation using two key scriptural proofs. Understanding this refutation is essential for defending the compatibility of divine mercy with divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice how the objection is framed: the Qur'an describes God as merciful, yet He creates disbelievers and punishes them eternally.
The objection uses verse 4:147 to highlight an apparent contradiction.
2
Identify the two counterarguments
The author gives two answers. Find the first one: citing verse 7:179. Then find the second one: referring to an earlier explanation in Surat al-Fatihah. How do these answer the objection?
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Ask yourself: does God's mercy mean He cannot punish anyone? Or is His mercy understood in a broader context, encompassing both temporal and eternal aspects? The commentary will help clarify.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السابع عشر للقوم
إن القرآن مملوء من كونه تعالى رحيماً كريماً جواداً محناً، بل الكتب الإلهية بأسرها مملؤة من هذا المعنى، والخلق مطبقون على كونه أكرم الأكرمين، وأرحم الراحمين من ذلك قوله تعالى: ( ما يفعل الله بعذابكم إن شكرتم ) فإن كان هو تعالى يخلق الإنسان ويسلط عليه في الدنيا الفقر والزمانة والمرض والعمى، ثم يخلق فيه الكفر، ثم يخرجه من الدنيا إلى الآخرة، ويدخله في النار أبد الآباد ويعذبه أشد العذاب . فكيف يليق به أن يكون كريماً رحيماً، فضلا عن أن يكون أكرم الأكرمين، وأرحم الراحمين ؟
واعلم : أن هذا نوع من الاستدلال، من أراد تطويله وتشعيبه قدر عليه. والجواب عنه إنه معارض بقوله: ( ولقد ذرأنا لجهنم كثيراً من الجن والإنس ) ثم الجواب عنه ما ذكرناه في سورة الفاتحة، في قوله: الرحمن الرحيم .
The seventeenth type of the people. Indeed the Qur'an is filled with that He, exalted, is Merciful, Generous, Munificent, Benign; rather all the divine books are filled with this meaning, and creation is unanimous upon His being the Most Generous of the generous and the Most Merciful of the merciful. From that is His saying, exalted: 'What would God do with your punishment if you are grateful?' (4:147). For if He, exalted, creates man and inflicts upon him in the world poverty, chronic illness, disease, and blindness, then creates in him disbelief, then takes him out from the world to the Hereafter, and enters him into the Fire for all eternity and punishes him with the most severe punishment, then how is it fitting for Him to be Generous and Merciful, let alone to be the Most Generous of the generous and the Most Merciful of the merciful? And know that this is a type of reasoning; whoever wishes to prolong it and branch it out is able to do so. And the answer to it is that it is countered by His saying: 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (7:179). Then the answer to it is what we mentioned in Surat al-Fatihah, in His saying: 'The Most Gracious, the Most Merciful' (1:1).
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage presents the seventeenth objection of the opponents (likely Mu'tazila or other rationalists) against the Ash'ari view that God creates all actions, including disbelief, and then punishes the disbeliever eternally. The author structures it as an objection-answer unit. 

First, he states the objection: The Qur'an and all divine books are filled with descriptions of God as Merciful, Generous, etc., and all people agree He is the Most Merciful and Most Generous. He cites the verse 'What would God do with your punishment if you are grateful?' (4:147) implying that punishment only occurs when ingratitude exists, but if God Himself creates the ingratitude, then punishment seems unjust. The objection then argues: If God creates man, afflicts him with poverty, chronic illness, disease, and blindness in this world, then creates disbelief in him, then takes him to the Hereafter and condemns him to eternal punishment in the Fire, how can He be called Merciful and Generous, let alone the Most Merciful and Most Generous?

Second, the author notes that this is a type of reasoning that one could elaborate if desired, but he provides a concise answer. The answer is twofold: 1) The objection is countered by another verse: 'And We have certainly created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (7:179), which explicitly states that God created some people with the ultimate destination of Hell. This shows that God's mercy is not incompatible with creating some for punishment. 2) The fuller answer is what was already explained in the commentary on Surat al-Fatihah regarding the names 'ar-Rahman ar-Raheem' (the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful), presumably indicating that God's mercy is inclusive of both believers and disbelievers in this world, but in the Hereafter it is specific to believers, and that His actions are just and wise beyond human comprehension.

The author thus refutes the objection by invoking scriptural authority and earlier theological explanation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this objection is unanswerable or that God's mercy logically contradicts eternal punishment, implying God is not truly merciful.
Correction: The author shows that the objection is answered by scripture (7:179) that states God created some for Hell, and by the theological principle that God's mercy in the Hereafter is specific to believers, while His justice is perfect.

Checkpoint
What is the primary scriptural counterargument the author gives against the objection that God's mercy is incompatible with creating disbelievers and punishing them eternally?
A) God's mercy is limited to this world only.
B) The verse (4:147) shows God does not punish the grateful.
C) The verse (7:179) states God created many for Hell, showing He may create some for punishment.
D) The objection is invalid because God is not truly merciful.

Key Takeaway
The objection that divine mercy is incompatible with creating disbelievers and eternally punishing them is refuted by scriptural evidence that God explicitly created some for Hell and by the nuanced understanding of God's mercy as both universal in this world and specific in the Hereafter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly accept that God's mercy is not diminished by His creating disbelievers for punishment, how does that change the way you pray for non-believers? Do you see them as recipients of God's mercy in this world, or only as objects of wrath?
?
Think of a moment when you questioned God's mercy because of suffering you saw. How does this lesson's answer—that mercy and justice coexist by divine wisdom—reshape your trust in God's character? What will you do the next time doubt arises?

Reconciling Divine Mercy with Predestination
The author presents an objection to God's mercy and gives a twofold Quranic answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author answers the objection that God's inflicting suffering and punishment contradicts His attribute of mercy.

Core Idea
God's mercy does not negate His justice; both are affirmed in the Quran.

This passage presents a powerful objection against God's mercy, then answers it with two Quranic references. The objection seems logical: if God creates people, afflicts them, creates disbelief in them, and then punishes them eternally, how can He be called 'Most Merciful'? The author shows that this objection is countered by Quranic verses that affirm God's decree and by a deeper understanding of His names Ar-Rahman and Ar-Rahim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection in the source passage. Notice how it builds from the Quran's emphasis on mercy to the apparent contradiction with punishment.
The objection is a common one; it's important to grasp its force.
2
Identify the two parts of the answer
The author gives two answers: first, a Quranic verse (7:179) that counters the objection; second, a reference to an earlier discussion on Ar-Rahman Ar-Rahim. Find both.
The second answer is not detailed here; it refers back to a prior explanation.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself: How does the verse 'We have created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' serve as a counter? What does it imply about God's mercy?
The answer lies in the fact that God's mercy and justice are not mutually exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع السابع عشر للقوم
إن القرآن مملوء من كونه تعالى رحيماً كريماً جواداً محناً ، بل الكتب الإلهية بأسرها مملؤة من هذا المعنى ، والخلق مطبقون على كونه أكرم الأكرمين ، وأرحم الراحمين من ذلك قوله تعالى : ( ما يفعل الله بعذابكم إن شكرتم ) فإن كان هو تعالى يخلق الإنسان ويسلط عليه في الدنيا الفقر والزمانة والمرض والعمى ، ثم يخلق فيه الكفر ، ثم يخرجه من الدنيا إلى الآخرة ، ويدخله في النار أبد الآباد ويعذبه أشد العذاب . فكيف يليق به أن يكون كريماً رحيماً ، فضلا عن أن يكون أكرم الأكرمين ، وأرحم الراحمين ؟
واعلم : أن هذا نوع من الاستدلال، من أراد تطويله وتشعيبه قدر عليه. والجواب عنه أنه معارض بقوله : ( ولقد ذرأنا لجهنم كثيراً من الجن والإنس ) ثم الجواب عنه ما ذكرناه في سورة الفاتحة ، في قوله : الرحمن الرحيم ) .
The seventeenth type for the people: Indeed the Quran is filled with Him, exalted, being merciful, generous, bountiful, beneficent; rather all the divine books are filled with this meaning, and creation is unanimous upon Him being the Most Generous of the generous and the Most Merciful of the merciful. Among that is His saying, exalted: 'What would God do with your punishment if you are grateful?' (4:147) So if He, exalted, creates the human and inflicts upon him in this world poverty, chronic illness, disease, and blindness, then creates disbelief in him, then takes him out of this world to the hereafter, and enters him into the Fire forever and ever and punishes him with the severest punishment, then how is it fitting for Him to be generous and merciful, let alone to be the Most Generous of the generous and the Most Merciful of the merciful? And know: this is a type of reasoning; whoever wishes to elaborate and branch it out is able to do so. The answer to it is that it is countered by His saying: 'And indeed We have created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (7:179). Then the answer to it is what we mentioned in Surah al-Fatihah, in His saying: 'Ar-Rahman Ar-Rahim'.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against God's mercy and provides a twofold answer. The objection begins by noting that the Quran and all divine books are filled with descriptions of God as merciful and generous, and that all of creation agrees that He is the Most Merciful. However, if we consider the actual course of events: God creates a human, afflicts him with poverty, disease, and blindness in this world, then creates disbelief in him, then takes his soul and casts him into eternal hellfire with the worst punishment—how can such actions befit a merciful and generous being, let alone the Most Merciful? The author then notes that this is a type of reasoning that could be elaborated further. The answer is given in two parts. First, the objection is countered by the Quranic verse 'And indeed We have created for Hell many of the jinn and mankind' (7:179), which indicates that God's creation of some for punishment does not contradict His mercy; rather, it is a manifestation of His wisdom and justice. Second, the author refers to an earlier discussion in Surah al-Fatihah regarding the divine names 'Ar-Rahman Ar-Rahim' (The Most Gracious, The Most Merciful), suggesting that the full explanation of how God's mercy is reconciled with His punishment has been laid out there. Thus, the objection is not left unanswered but is grounded in Quranic texts and prior theological reasoning.

الرحيم

الكتب الإلهية

Misconception: God's eternal punishment contradicts His mercy, making Him not truly merciful.
Correction: The passage shows that God's mercy is not negated by punishment; both are affirmed in the Quran and reconciled through divine wisdom and justice.

Key Takeaway
God's mercy and justice are not contradictory; both are affirmed in the Quran, and apparent conflicts are resolved through His wisdom and revealed texts.

?
